==PAGE:4==
ii
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
This master thesis investigates the roles of power and trust on collaborative buyer-
supplier dyadic relationships. The existing research gap concerning the concurrent im-
pact of these two factors on paired buyer-supplier relationships triggered the research
concerning this particular topic. In order to address the research questions, a buying
firm belonging to the oil and gas sector was used as a case company, and an analysis of
five strategic suppliers of this company was carried out for this purpose. To tackle the
objectives of this project, a thorough literature review of relevant studies was conducted,
followed by the development of a conceptual framework and the selection of the case
company. Next, 10 interviews were executed with stakeholders from both the buying
firm and the supplier companies. For the analysis of the interview data, thematic quali-
tative analysis principles were used. Comparing the views of the relationship dimensions
of the buying firm and the suppliers, it was found that there are significant differences
in their perceptions, in particular regarding power asymmetry and the most powerful
trading partner, the exercise of power in the relationship, the impact and existence of
trust in the relationship and the overall assessment of supply chain collaboration. These
findings have some important theoretical implications, supporting but also contradicting
some of the literature studies reviewed for this project, as well as practical implications
that can help managers optimize their supplier relationship management efforts.
Keywords:
Power, trust, buyer-supplier dyads, supply chain collaboration, perceptions, partners,
supplier relationship management, oil and gas industry

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Background & Motivation
In the past decade, firms have been looking outside their organizational boundaries for
opportunities to collaborate with supply chain partners to ensure the efficiency and
responsiveness of their supply chains, so as to leverage the resources and knowledge of
their suppliers and customers (Fawcett & Magnan 2004). This collaboration fostered
information and knowledge sharing within supply chains and allowed firms to access
data across their supply chains, enabling them to collaborate in activities such as sales,
production, and logistics and ultimately enhance the supply chain performance through
integrated information networks (Cao & Zhang 2011, Handfield & Bechtel 2002, Lorentz
et al. 2011, Simatupang & Sridharan 2002). From a theoretical point of view, there
is an increasing research body focusing on buyer-supplier relationship interactions in
business-to-business contexts, and even more so in the procurement and supply chain
field.
When looking to identify relevant inter-organizational factors and understand
how their interplay affect firm-level performance outcomes, three of the most relevant
factors mentioned in the current supply chain management literature are supply chain
collaboration, trading partner power and trading partner trust (Ambrose et al. 2010,
Cuevas et al. 2015, Jain et al. 2014, Khoja et al. 2011, Mora-Monge et al. 2019).
Despite the wealth of research on power and trust and their impact on supply chain
collaboration and overall performance, there are still some unanswered questions. First,
since both the power and the trust mechanisms do not occur in isolation, their mutual
effect on supply chain collaboration and supply chain performance, as well as the inter-
play between them needs to be further clarified. Moreover, it was considered insightful
to examine buyer-supplier dyads, where one player remains the same (case study of the
relationships of a buying firm or a supplier firm), as most of the studies so far are inves-
tigating non-dyadic buyer-supplier relationships. To further narrow the research field,
the Oil and Gas sector was used as an industry case study.
1.2
Research Question & Objectives
Consequently, this project attempted to answer the overarching research question of:
==PAGE:20==
2
Introduction
How do power asymmetry and trust act as key drivers affecting the performance of col-
laborative dyadic buyer-supplier relationships in the oil and gas sector and what is their
respective impact on each other?
This can be further broken down into the following sub-questions:
1. How does power affect supply chain collaboration?
2. How does trust affect supply chain collaboration?
3. How does trust affect the use/perception of power in a business relationship?
4. How does power affect the presence/type of trust developed in a business relation-
ship?
5. Which components of SCC are present in collaborative supply chains and how do
they affect the overall collaboration?
6. What is the effect of SCC on the overall supply chain performance?
Apart from the research question, several learning objectives were set out in the
beginning of this project. These were the following:
• Identify and critically reflect on relevant scientific and engineering knowledge
within the effects of trust and power in supplier-buyer relationship management
based on research literature.
• Identify relevant scientific problems within trust and power in buyer-supplier rela-
tionship management and formulate clear and relevant research questions.
• Identify and review the relevant scientific literature and based on a literature re-
view, develop a conceptual framework guiding the empirical work.
• Assess, select and apply appropriate scientific methods, tools and competencies
within supplier-buyer relationship management.
• Analyse, explain and discuss relevant professional and scientific problems.
• Describe the findings of this work and critically discuss these in the light of the
literature and the conceptual framework that guided the empirical work.
• Manage work and development situations that are complex and unforeseen and
require new solution models.
• Independently initiate and carry out discipline-specific and cross-disciplinary co-
operation and to assume professional responsibility.
• Disseminate research-based knowledge as well as the final project approach and
outcome.
==PAGE:21==
1.3 Thesis Outline
3
• Provide proper documentation, including proper referencing throughout the project
and provide a complete reference list.
In order to achieve the above goals, the research methodology that was followed
was: (i) to conduct an extensive literature review of relevant academic sources, (ii) to
develop a conceptual framework that best fits the research questions, (iii) to identify a
case company serving as an industry example to explore the variables and aspects of the
framework, (iv) to conduct semi-structured interviews, and (v) to analyse the interview
data by applying qualitative thematic analysis tools, in order to obtain the final findings
of the study.
1.3
Thesis Outline
The remainder of this report is structured as described below:
Chapter 2: Literature Review & Conceptual
Development
In this chapter, a structured and thorough analysis of the extant literature on the topics
of supply chain collaboration, trust and power are presented, with the aim to identify
their main constructs as well as their effects on each other and on supply chain perfor-
mance. Next, the conceptual framework that was developed is presented, illustrating
these relationships and the effects between trust, power, supply chain collaboration and
supply chain performance.
Chapter 3: Research Approach
This chapter outlines the research methodology that was followed. First, there is an
introduction of the case study, followed by a detailed description of the data collection
and data analysis methods used.
Chapter 4: Findings
This chapter presents the findings of the analysis of the interview data, supported by
quotes taken verbatim from the interview transcripts.
==PAGE:22==
4
Introduction
Chapter 5: Discussion
Important remarks on the implications and interpretations of the findings of this research
project, as well as their connection to the literature and research questions are discussed
in this chapter.
Chapter 6: Limitations & Suggestions for Future Research
The limitations of this study are outlined in this chapter and some recommendations
are made for future research on this subject.
Chapter 7: Conclusion
In the last chapter of the report, the major conclusions of the thesis are presented.
Bibliography
A list of all the scientific papers and reports that were utilized for this project report.
Appendices
The appendices of this report consist of the project plan that was created in the beginning
of the project together with an evaluation of it, supporting tables that include the most
relevant quotes from the interviews and the complete interview transcripts.
==PAGE:23==
CHAPTER 2
Literature Review &
Conceptual Development
With the dawn of the new millennium, supply chain management (SCM) has progressed
from a narrow emphasis on procurement and logistics during the 90’s to a wider, more
holistic emphasis on value creation (Chopra et al. 2013). Supply chain excellence is
increasingly seen by leading businesses as more than just a means of cost savings - they
see it as a source of competitive advantage, with potential for enhancing customer ser-
vice efficiency, increasing revenue streams, maximizing resource utilization and reducing
waste (Corsten & Kumar 2005, Sridharan & Simatupang 2013). For the achievement of
these goals, successful cooperation within each organization (intra-organisational) and
between chain partners (inter-organisational) is necessary. This has led to increasing
efforts in both the acedemia and the industry, to identify and implement those factors
that can foster successful collaboration in supply chains.
From a theoretical point of view, there is an increasing research body focusing on
buyer-supplier relationship interactions in business-to-business contexts, and even more
so in the procurement and supply chain field. In this project, the main focus was placed
on the effects of power and trust on supply chain collaboration.
Therefore, in this
chapter, a structured and thorough analysis of the extant literature on these topics will
be presented, with the aim to identify their main constructs as well as their effects on
each other and on supply chain performance.
To gather the initial database of scientific research papers, an extensive research
was conducted, using primarily three search engines, namely “Google Scholar”, “Web of
Science” and “DTU Findit”. The keywords that were used were: supplier relationship
management, supply chain collaboration, buyer-supplier relationship management, inter-
firm relationship, trust, power, power asymmetry, procurement and others. An initial
database of 86 research papers was identified, and was then screened by reviewing the
titles and the abstracts. Some of the inclusion criteria were relevance with the topic,
significance of the research findings, literature review relevance and methodology. Then,
more papers were identified as relevant by reviewing some commonly cited papers in the
obtained database. The final database consisted of 130 research papers on the before
mentioned topics. Figure 2.1 depicts a word-cloud, which was formed by collecting the
keywords of the 130 research papers in the literature database to illustrate the focus on
the specific areas of this research project.
Finally, the conceptual framework that was developed will be presented, illustrating
==PAGE:24==
6
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
Figure 2.1. An illustration of the literature review keywords used in this project.
these relationships and the effects between trust, power, supply chain collaboration and
supply chain performance.
2.1
Supply Chain Collaboration
In recent decades, companies have had to look outside of their organizations for opportu-
nities to work with partners to make sure the supply chain is efficient and responsive to
dynamic market needs. Companies have sought greater collaboration in the supply chain
to leverage their suppliers and customers’ resources and knowledge (Fawcett & Magnan
2004). Supply chain collaboration includes two or more separate companies working
together to coordinate and carry out supply chain operations (Simatupang & Sridharan
2002) which may provide significant benefits to the partners (Lorentz et al. 2011). For
instance, in order to reduce transaction costs and gain a stronger competitive position
(Handfield & Bechtel 2002), companies such as Procter & Gamble and IBM have formed
long-term relationships with their suppliers. Some of the most common collaborative
objectives are to improve overall business performance, share risks, access additional
resources (Kim et al. 2010), reduce transaction costs, improve profitability as well as
forecast accuracy (Lorentz et al. 2011, Simatupang & Sridharan 2002), thereby improv-
ing income efficiency and competitive advantage over time (Handfield & Bechtel 2002,
Simatupang & Sridharan 2002).
First, it should be noted that Supply Chain Collaboration (SCC) and Supply Chain
Integration (SCI) were often used interchangeably as both apply to a close pairing pro-
==PAGE:25==
2.1 Supply Chain Collaboration
7
cess between Supply Chain Partners. The term integration, however, means the central-
ized control (or ownership) of many related processes which were previously carried out
independently (Zhang & Huo 2013, Zhao et al. 2008). It thus places greater focus on
centralized control, ownership or contractually controlled process integration. Accord-
ing to the transaction cost economics (TCE) theory, collaboration is an intermediate
type of hybrid governance between the two ends of the management spectrum of ver-
tical integration and market exchange (Bakos & Brynjolfsson 1993, Poppo et al. 2016).
Although there are overlaps between SCC and SCI, it is easier for SCC to capture the
shared relationship between the autonomous supply chain partners (Nyaga et al. 2010).
2.1.1
Defining Supply Chain Collaboration
Several authors have described the supply chain collaboration in different ways. Ta-
ble 2.1 contains a summary of the various available definitions, and it was created by
chronologically ranking the most cited definitions of SCC in the obtained database of
studies.
Overall it can be argued that the different definitions of supply chain collaboration
can typically be divided into two conceptualization groups: process integration focus
and relationship focus.
Process Integration Focus
A business process through which two or more supply chain partners work together
towards common goals (Cao & Zhang 2011, Fawcett & Magnan 2004, Simatupang
& Sridharan 2002, Sridharan & Simatupang 2013).
Relationship Focus
Forming close, long-term partnerships where supply chain members work together
and share information, resources, and risk to achieve mutual goals (Cai et al. 2013,
Cao & Zhang 2011, Hudnurkar et al. 2014, Wang et al. 2016).
The literature review shows the importance of organizing activities (Mora-Monge
et al. 2019), incorporating cross-functional processes, integrating the supply chain (Zhang
& Huo 2013), setting expectations for the supply chain (Samaddar & Kadiyala 2006),
and setting criteria for sharing of information (Baihaqi & Sohal 2013, Hudnurkar et al.
2014, Simatupang & Sridharan 2002). Combining both the process and the relationship
emphasis, SCC can be characterized as a collaboration process in which two or more
independent firms work together to plan and execute supply chain operations towards
shared goals and mutual benefits.
Hence, depending on the objectives of the researchers, the two theories that have
been utilised the most when studying the effects of SCC in supply chain performance
or overall business performance are the Transaction Cost Economics theory (TCE) and
the Resource Dependence Theory (RDT). Through the TCE lenses, SCC is viewed as
==PAGE:26==
8
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
a tool that enables firms to reduce the negative consequences of opportunism that are
implicitly involved in business transactions, by fostering mutual trust and integrating
processes between the trading partners. Thus, the probability that partners will behave
in the best interests of the relationship is increased (Buvik & Reve 2002). On the other
hand, RDT argues that investing in relationship-specific assets helps partnering firms to
gain competitive advantage due to their unique, important, non-replaceable and hard-to-
imitate nature (Buvik & Reve 2002). SCC also helps companies to concentrate on their
particular core operations, which improve firm-specific expertise and realize economies
of scale and learning impacts, thereby strengthening their competitive positions (Park
et al. 2010).
Table 2.1. Definitions of Supply Chain Collaboration in the literature.
Source
Definition
Simatupang et al. (2004)
Collaboration is a cooperative strategy of supply chain part-
ners with a common goal of serving customer through inte-
grated solutions for lowering cost and increasing revenue.
Kampstra et al. (2006)
Financially independent entities try to get the dependent
parts of the chain to “play” together, i.e. ensuring that
the entities in a chain interact successfully to provide the
necessary coordinated outputs.
Fawcett et al. (2008)
The ability to work across organizational boundaries to
build and manage unique value-added processes to better
meet customer needs.
Simatupang & Sridharan
(2008)
Collaboration describes the cooperation among indepen-
dent, but related firms to share resources and capabilities to
meet their customers’ most extraordinary or dynamically
changing needs.
Whipple et al. (2010)
Supply chain collaboration is a long-term relationship
where participants generally cooperate, share information,
and work together to plan and even modify their business
practices to improve joint performance.
Cao & Zhang (2011)
A partnership process where two or more autonomous firms
work closely to plan and execute supply chain operations
toward common goals and mutual benefits.
Sridharan & Simatupang
(2013)
Supply chain collaboration starts with communicating ob-
jectives, plans, and forecasts to working closely with part-
ners to create efficient flows of products to end customers.
In any collaborative efforts, open communication to other
chain members means taking a risk of vulnerability to the
actions of another party.
==PAGE:27==
2.1 Supply Chain Collaboration
9
2.1.2
Issues, effects and factors affecting Supply Chain
Collaboration
In this section the extant literature on supply chain collaboration will be analyzed, by
presenting a brief description of the reviewed studies and their key findings. Special
emphasis is given towards the identified issues affecting the success of SCC, the overall
effects SCC has on supply chain and firm performance, as well as the identification of
the factors that promote and enable successful collaborative supply chains.
In one of the first studies regarding SCC, Simatupang & Sridharan (2002) argued
that the mutual benefits of SCC are rarely realised by all the trading partners. They
suggested that the collaborative supply chain should continuously consider information
sharing and incentive alignment in line with relevant performance metrics and coordi-
nated strategies as measures to minimize the adverse effects of managerial bias on supply
chain performance. In line with this previous observation, the paper by Simatupang et al.
(2004) attempts to apply the Theory of Constraints in order to address the difficulties of
understanding the possible advantages of SCC, by highlighting the underlying difficulty
of collaboration, namely the adaptation of decisions that take into account the interests
of the supply chain as a whole and the protection of the interests of individual firms.
Recognizing that the collaborating participants are rarely able to see all potential collab-
orative benefits, Lorentz et al. (2011) suggests a framework to evaluate the performance
of companies involved in initial and advanced stages of the collaboration. Fawcett et al.
(2008) applied problems contingency theory and force field theory with the goal to study
effectively overcome the cultural and technical barriers to SCC.
Whipple et al. (2010) compare collaborative and transactional relationships in buy-
ing companies to assess whether collaborative relationships provide greater benefits than
transactional relationships, and their findings indicate that collaborative relationships
provide higher levels of satisfaction and success than transactional ones. In the same
spirit, the objective of the study by Cao & Zhang (2011) was to uncover the essence
of supply chain collaboration and explore its effect on firm performance based on a col-
laborative advantage paradigm, while the aim of the study by Patrucco et al. (2019)
was to investigate the dynamics of the buyer – supplier relationships involved in collab-
orative projects. Their findings support the importance of collaborative partnerships in
improving performance, highlighting the key role that the buying firm’s procurement
department can play in managing the different elements included in supplier collabora-
tion.
Cai et al. (2013) attempt to interpret the processes underlying the knowledge sharing
in dyadic buyer-supplier relationships, noting that trust and power are two important
precedents of two forms of knowledge sharing between buyer and supplier, namely techni-
cal exchange and technology transfer. Sridharan & Simatupang (2013) offer a conceptual
framework to understand how trust and power interact and exert influence over value
creation and appropriation as the outcomes of supply chain collaboration. Nyaga et al.
(2013) in their study examine how perceptions of power use affect behavioral and opera-
tional outcomes and prevailing relationship quality in dyadic relationships characterized
==PAGE:28==
10
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
by substantial power asymmetry. Results indicate that power use affects partner be-
havior and operational performance, but the nature of the relationship dictates which
power sources are most appropriate.
The study by Nyaga et al. (2010) aimed at finding the factors that promote successful
collaborations and the results showed that collaborative activities, such as information
sharing, joint relationship effort, and dedicated investments contribute to trust and
commitment, which in turn lead to improved satisfaction and performance. Samaddar
& Kadiyala (2006) argued that efficient allocation and sharing of resources is important
for any successful collaboration, and discussed the conditions for resource sharing in
which organizations are encouraged to work together towards knowledge creation, as well
as the conditions for continued collaboration. In the work by Kampstra et al. (2006),
the authors highlight the need for an effective supply chain plan and well-articulated
objectives, arguing that such an approach enables the stakeholders involved to improve
the efficiency of their supply chains. Lee & Ha (2018) find that both cognitive and
structural capital theories indicate that buyers consider that they may obtain sufficient
information from their suppliers, but are often unwilling to provide their suppliers with
information themselves.
2.1.3
The components of successful Supply Chain
Collaboration
Synthesizing the literature, there are seven interconnecting components that together
form the principle of successful supply chain collaboration, namely information sharing,
joint knowledge creation, resources sharing, decision synchronization, collaborative com-
munication, goal congruence and incentive alignment between independent supply chain
partners (Cao & Zhang 2011, Nyaga et al. 2010). These seven components are consid-
ered to be interrelated and co-variate with each other while causal explanations can
exist between them. Collectively, they are considered to contribute positively to SCC
by optimizing resource utilization, lowering costs, enhancing innovation and increasing
supply chain responsiveness. Below, these components are described in more detail.
Information sharing is defined as the degree to which an organization exchanges
with its supply chain partners a variety of appropriate, accurate, and complete informa-
tion in a timely manner (Baihaqi & Sohal 2013, Hudnurkar et al. 2014, Lee & Ha 2018,
Simatupang & Sridharan 2002). Tactical information such as inventory levels, forecasts,
sales and marketing promotion strategies are the most common information being ex-
changed among supply chain partners. Some scholars have described information sharing
as the foundation and most important requirement of supply chain collaboration (Bai-
haqi & Sohal 2013, Hudnurkar et al. 2014).
Joint knowledge creation refers to the degree to which supply chain partners
work together to gain a deeper understanding of and response to demand and the com-
petitive environment (Samaddar & Kadiyala 2006). Knowledge exploration (i.e. the
search for and acquisition of new and relevant information) and knowledge exploitation
==PAGE:29==
2.1 Supply Chain Collaboration
11
(i.e. knowledge assimilation and application) are the two forms of knowledge creation
activities most commonly cited in the literature (Harland et al. 2004). According to
the same study, capturing, sharing, and assimilating information (e.g. process, technol-
ogy, or business awareness) between supply chain partners, facilitates innovation and
long-term sustainability of the supply chain as a whole.
Resource sharing refers to the process of optimizing resources and assets and
investing in capacity and assets with supply chain partners. These resources can include
both physical resources, such as production equipment, facilities and technologies, and
human resources (Harland et al. 2004). An indicative example of this practice found
primarily in the retail sector is the vendor-managed inventory (VMI), which allows
suppliers to analyze stock-level data through Electronic Data Interchange (EDI) and
take the required replenishment action (Cao & Zhang 2011).
Decision synchronization refers to a framework through which supply chain part-
ners plan and execute decisions in supply chain management that maximize the supply
chain benefits (Simatupang & Sridharan 2002). Planning decisions are made to decide
how to use a company’s resources most efficiently and effectively to accomplish a partic-
ular set of objectives. In general, there are seven key supply chain management decision
planning categories, namely: demand planning, production planning and scheduling,
operations strategy planning, procurement, delivery on promise, change balancing, and
distribution management (Chopra et al. 2013). Joint planning is used to coordinate
strategic stakeholders and to make operational decisions, including replenishment of
inventory and placement and delivery of orders.
Collaborative communication can be regarded as the effective communication
system and methods of collaboration for supply chain partners working towards com-
mon goals or objectives. The attributes of collaborative communication are frequency,
direction, mode, and influence strategy. Open, regular, structured, two-way, multilevel
communication is typically an indicator of close relationships between organizations (Cao
& Zhang 2011, Maestrini et al. 2018).
Goal congruence is the extent to which supply chain partners consider their own
goals being achieved by achieving the supply chain goals. This is the degree of goal
agreement between supply chain partners (Cuevas et al. 2015, Kim et al. 2010). In the
case of true goal congruence, supply chain partners either assume that their priorities
align perfectly with those of the supply chain, or, in the case of discrepancies, believe
that their goals can be accomplished as an immediate result of working towards the
supply chain priorities (Cuevas et al. 2015).
Incentive alignment refers to the system of cost, benefit and risk sharing between
supply chain partners (Simatupang & Sridharan 2008, Simatupang et al. 2004). This
requires the assessment of costs, risks, and advantages, and the design of incentives.
Effective supply chain collaborations require that each participant has an even share
of gains and losses and that the results of the collaboration benefit all participants
unarguably (Simatupang & Sridharan 2002, Sridharan & Simatupang 2013). Incentive
alignment requires careful interpretation of profit-sharing structures, ensuring benefits
are directly proportional to investment and risk (Kampstra et al. 2006).
Apart from these components, the extant literature indicates that the overall success
==PAGE:30==
12
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
of a collaborative supply chain heavily depends on the level of trust between the trading
partners (Nyaga et al. 2010, Whipple et al. 2010), as well as the existence or perception
of power asymmetry in the exchange relationship (Chicksand 2015, Rehme et al. 2016,
Wang et al. 2016). These two key aspects of the relationships are further discussed in
the following sections 2.2 and 2.3, highlighting their respective effects on SCC as well as
their interaction, as seen through the lenses of the currently existing academic research.
2.1.4
The impact of Supply Chain Collaboration on
Supply Chain Performance
The primary goal of introducing collaboration in any supply chain is to improve the
overall performance of the supply chain, and this can be accomplished by the collective
efforts of all supply chain partners. The rationale behind this lies heavily in the antici-
pated improvements in performance, agility and a competitive advantage on the market,
with many studies suggesting that improved supply chain and overall business perfor-
mance are linked to collaborative relationships (Cao & Zhang 2011, Doney & Cannon
1997, Giannakis 2007, Mora-Monge et al. 2019, Nyaga et al. 2010).
Daugherty et al. (2006) found that companies involved in collaborative relationships
achieved improved visibility, increased service levels, increased flexibility, increased
end-customer satisfaction and reduced cycle times.
Ampe-N’DA et al. (2020) argue
that, through successful supplier relationships, businesses are offered the opportunity to
shorten lead times and product life cycles, thereby allowing them to maintain a compet-
itive edge. Therefore, as the world becomes increasingly globalized, collaboration is a
vital element of success for any organization with a desire to expand and develop.
Important specifics, however, are often ignored, such as choosing the right partner,
matching needs and capabilities across organisations, and clearly identifying priorities
and objectives. In addition, questions arise as to whether the collaboration benefits both
buyers and suppliers. In a research project of collaborative partnerships between suppli-
ers and large retailers, Corsten & Kumar (2005) found that while both parties benefited
from the collaboration, suppliers felt greater sense of inequity in the relationship. This
indicates that there is skepticism among suppliers regarding equality in relationships.
Nevertheless, there’s no final answer to this problem and it’s hard to identify the best
way to invest in long-term supplier relationships.
Synthesizing the literature, most studies agree that effective supply chain col-
laboration leads to collaborative advantage and better firm performance (Ampe-N’DA
et al. 2020, Cao & Zhang 2011, Daugherty et al. 2006, Doney & Cannon 1997, Mora-
Monge et al. 2019, Nyaga et al. 2010). In order to ensure that the supply chain as a
whole performs well, firms should try to create a win-win situation. Long-term relation-
ships must be motivated by the mutual motives, goal congruence, and sharing of benefits
(Simatupang & Sridharan 2002). According to Carr & Pearson (1999), managers need
to align goals and benefits with supply chain partners for creating a collaborative ad-
vantage, which is found to directly increase the financial performance for each partner
==PAGE:31==
2.2 Trust
13
in the chain. It can also be an effective means of transferring knowledge and new tech-
nical skills across firms. Last but not least, it is worth noting that a firm may have a
better chance of accomplishing its objective of acquiring new skills and competencies by
collaborating with a firm that is exemplary in that area (Cao & Zhang 2011).
2.2
Trust
As previously mentioned in 2.1, trust plays an extremely important role in collaborative
supply chains, with many scholars naming it the “foundation” of SCC and one of the
most important antecedents of every successful business relationship (Cai et al. 2013,
Handfield & Bechtel 2002, Sridharan & Simatupang 2013). In the following sections, a
large body of literature on trust was analyzed, with an emphasis on the effect of trust
on supply chain collaboration and exchange relationships between trading partners.
2.2.1
Definition of trust
In order to better understand the term “trust” and its different aspects, it is essential
to attempt to interpret/define it.
There are several definitions of trust available in
many different disciplines like philosophy, social psychology, economics, contract law,
and marketing, trying to define the inherently broad and complex nature of the term.
Each discipline offers unique insights into the nature of trust, its interpretation, and its
development processes (Doney & Cannon 1997).
Moorman et al. (1992) define trust as “a willingness to rely on an exchange partner
in whom one has confidence”. Caniëls et al. (2010) and Ambrose et al. (2010) support
this by also adding that trust can only exist when there is confidence in the reliability
and integrity of the exchange partner. On a similar note, the empirical study by Pa-
paroidamis et al. (2019) treats trust as “the extent to which an organizational customer
is confident in the credibility and benevolence of its supplying partner”. Thus, when look-
ing at an exchange relationship, trust is the firm’s willingness to accept being vulnerable
on the basis of positive expectations of the trading partner’s behavior in the business
association (Jain et al. 2014, Mora-Monge et al. 2019).
The definition that is considered to better represent the term in the context of supply
chain management - and will, therefore, be in mind for the remainder of this project -
comes from Anderson & Narus (1990). For them, trust reflects a firm’s confidence that
another organization would engage in activities that would result in favorable outcomes
for the business and would not take unforeseen steps that will result in negative results for
the firm. Therefore, in assessing the existence or lack of trust in an exchange relationship,
both positive and negative outcomes are taken into account with equal focus.
==PAGE:32==
14
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
2.2.2
Types of trust
According to the literature, there are also different types of trust. These types mainly
refer to the different foundation based upon trust is built or perceived between the
trading partners.
For instance, literature distinguishes between goodwill trust and competence
trust (Caniëls et al. 2010). Goodwill trust is based upon the belief that both partners
are interested in each other’s benefit/progress, and that they will not engage themselves
in actions that would potentially harm their partner in any way (Anderson & Narus 1990,
Geyskens et al. 1998). On the other hand, competence trust is based upon the objective
credibility of a trading partner. In other words, it refers to the confidence that since a
partner is in possession of high technical expertise and expertness, they will therefore
act and perform according to the established agreements between the trading partners
(Caniëls et al. 2010). Competence trust is also found with other names in literature,
such as “cognitive trust” or “cognitive-based trust”. However, cognitive-based trust is
perceived to include a few more aspects in it, such as the reliability and dependability of
the trading partner, and hence can sometimes be differentiated from competence trust
(Mayer et al. 1995).
Another type of trust commonly cited in the literature is normative/affective trust
(Dadzie et al. 2018, Handfield & Bechtel 2002, Jain et al. 2014, Mayer et al. 1995,
Rogers & Fells 2017). Normative trust is a judgment anchored in past behavior and
characterized by a shared identity between partners. This type of trust is associated with
high levels of commitment in the relationship and is based on the feelings of affiliation,
identification, and loyalty (Gounaris 2005, Jain et al. 2014).
The last type of trust identified in the literature is calculative trust (Jain et al.
2014, Poppo et al. 2016). Calculative trust is typically founded on a system of incen-
tives and penalties in the relationship between the trading partners, making it more
realistic in nature. Expectations are analyzed by intentionally and pragmatically ex-
amining future situations, including cost-benefit considerations and depending on the
relative importance of cheating and cooperation. If calculative trust is high, parties
assume that cooperation and performance goals will be accomplished since failure to do
so, will result in punitive measures, including termination of the exchange relationship.
Therefore penalties and rewards minimize opportunistic behavior, control interactions,
and preserve cooperation.
2.2.3
The constructs of trust
Being such a broad and complex term, trust has a number of key components that to-
gether constitute the term. Rogers & Fells (2017) identified different dimensions of trust
that emerged through the interviews with supply chain and procurement professionals.
First, trust was seen as being developed out of someone’s personal performance over
time. Moreover, trust seemed to be about the approach each individual takes to deal
with problems, such as fairness and openness. Frequent communication with counter-
==PAGE:33==
2.2 Trust
15
parts, for example, offers opportunities to build trust (openness), while fairness is re-
garded as both parties getting something out of the relationship, which also contributes
to reciprocal commitment. With that in mind, trust can be viewed as an element of
personal integrity. When the parties identify with one another and acknowledge each
other’s needs and goals, a deeper degree of trust comes into play (identification-based
trust).
Undoubtedly, trust is deemed to be very important for business relationships, but
also easily assumed. Trust should be founded upon the honesty of an individual. Lastly,
the trust between buyer and supplier will be mainly self-interested and calculative in
the first phases of forming an exchange relationship, but even for this minimal degree of
trust to be established, there must be certainty (Rogers & Fells 2017).
All in all, the most frequently mentioned constructs of trust in literature are depen-
dence, rewards/penalties, integrity, reliability, competence, closeness, openness, secu-
rity, and credibility. These can further be grouped to reflect the types of trust discussed
above. A suggested grouping could be as illustrated in Figure 2.2. It can, therefore, be
argued that the basic constructs of calculative trust are dependence and the existence of
rewards/penalties. Similarly for cognitive-based trust, integrity, reliability, and compe-
tence are the most important constructs. Lastly, for normative/affective trust the basic
constructs are closeness, openness, and security/credibility. These are supported by the
review of the extant literature presented in 2.2.2.
Figure 2.2. The different types and constructs of trust.
==PAGE:34==
16
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
2.2.4
The effect of trust in supply chain
There has been a substantial amount of research focusing on the effects of trust in
exchange relationships. Focusing more on the supply chain context, trust has often been
seen as a vital mechanism in the inter-organizational framework to facilitate coordination
and collaboration among trading partners (Mora-Monge et al. 2019).
However, this
impact of trust is yet considered ambiguous.
The trust literature offers theoretical reasoning that underlies the assumption that
organizational customers who trust their suppliers will remain loyal to the exchange re-
lationship. It has been proposed that trusting business organizations enable the achieve-
ment of improved outcomes by increasing the value of transactions and reducing the
transaction costs of inter-organizational exchanges (Cai et al. 2013, Paparoidamis et al.
2019, Poppo et al. 2016, Whipple et al. 2010). Research indicates that trusting firms
experience a reduction in transaction costs as they spend less time and effort on, and
commit fewer resources to, resolving issues, tracking each other’s performance of one
another, and implementing agreements. In particular, trust enables partner companies
to engage in collaborative activities by exchanging valuable information and expertise,
contributing to joint projects and, in effect, orchestrating their needs and ways of work-
ing. Trusted partners make effective use of valuable knowledge as each party contributes
openly important information that facilitates the smooth functioning of their relation-
ship exchanges. Partnering companies thus engage more productively and cope more
efficiently with day-to-day problems, which in turn increases the value of transactions
in the relationship.
The study by Lee & Ha (2018) used the relational, social, and cognitive capital
theories, and the findings suggest that mutual trust and reciprocity in relationships can
be key to fostering collaborative and long-term exchange relationships by preventing
information asymmetry from occurring in the relationship. In the study by Rogers &
Fells (2017), the participants were asked about three primary factors contributing to
successful long-term relationships, and trust seemed to be central to these. Zhao et al.
(2008), Yeung et al. (2009) and Zhang & Huo (2013) support that there is clear evidence
that effective supply chain partnerships generally depend largely on trust because of
their ability to reduce exchange risks, such as uncertainty and opportunism, while at
the same time fostering information sharing, cost savings and increased willingness of the
trading partners to collaborate outside contractual requirements. Handfield & Bechtel
(2002) and Doney & Cannon (1997) argue that trust can have a significant impact on the
responsiveness of supply chains, allowing a deeper understanding of supplier performance
and capacity constraints, improving communication and information sharing (leading to
more accurate forecasting), enhancing proactive problem resolution and paving the way
for Business-to-Business (B2B) e-commerce applications.
The study by Gorton et al. (2015) indicates that greater buyer trustworthiness has
an important, beneficial impact on the supplier’s satisfaction with the partnership and
their performance in terms of both quantity and quality of product. This reinforces
the idea that increased rates of customer opportunism hinder the ability of suppliers to
==PAGE:35==
2.3 Power
17
make an extra effort or to engage in investments, and thereby serves as a constraint on
improvements in the quality and quantity of production from suppliers. Such a finding
thus inhibits efforts to improve profitability and market competitiveness (Handfield &
Bechtel 2002). Zhang & Huo (2013) add that in fact, the majority of scientific evidence
points to the positive impacts of trust on the multiple dimensions of supply chain man-
agement, such as the integration of customers and suppliers, the duration and scope
of cooperation between trading partners and the overall successful cooperation between
them.
Despite the above-mentioned trust benefits, some researchers recognized a “secret”
side of trust in inter-organizational contexts and observed an inverted U-shaped rela-
tionship between trust and business performance. Evidence seems to indicate that if the
trust between trading partners exceeds a certain point, it can negate its potential bene-
fits and, in turn, lead to a decline in performance (Mora-Monge et al. 2019, Yeung et al.
2009). This decrease in business performance can be explained by the over-optimistic
and subjective view of a trading partner, which can lead to dysfunctional mechanisms
such as reduction of monitoring, aversion to conflict, opportunism, and discriminatory
treatment of potentially better trading partners, leading to higher costs and lower rev-
enues.
In the empirical study by Caniëls et al. (2010), the authors quantified the changes in
mutual dependence and trust/commitment in various relationship development stages.
The hypotheses they formulated were largely confirmed. More precisely, they argue that
as buyers and suppliers develop and extend their relationship, their connection intensifies,
and the perceived mutual dependency, as well as trust/commitment increases until an
enhanced partnership, is achieved. When the business relationship becomes undesirable,
the positive pattern becomes negative, with commitment and trust declining rapidly,
while the dependence of the supplier begins to break. The dependence of the buyer
remains relatively constant and weakens only slightly as the relationship reaches the
final stage. In general, the simple existence of asymmetric positions of dependency in
relationships is linked with instability and conflict (Anderson & Narus 1990, Geyskens
et al. 1998). However, Caniëls et al. (2010) argue that asymmetry of dependence does
not necessarily have to be bad for a relationship. Even though trust and dependence are
influencing the bargaining power of firms, trust could act as a moderating mechanism to
counteract the potential negative effects of dependence asymmetry on the relationship.
2.3
Power
As stated in 2.1, power is central to supply chain relationships. How to gain and use
power with other parties in the supply chain is a frequent concern in management prac-
tice, and an even bigger issue in strategic collaborative relationships, where power asym-
metry plays an important role in relationship evolution and longevity. Power asymmetry
results from one company needing the resources of another company more than the other.
By selectively withholding or granting access to its resources in exchange for favorable
==PAGE:36==
18
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
terms, the more powerful company can make use of its power. The degree to which
this power difference is exploited depends on factors such as how likely it is that the
difference will erode over time and whether it can be controlled by the executives. It is
intuitive that the ability to use power, as well as the actual use of power, is to the benefit
of the more powerful companies and to the disadvantage of the less powerful company.
In this section, a large body of literature on power was analyzed, with an emphasis on
its impact on supply chain collaboration and exchange relationships between trading
partners.
2.3.1
Definition of power
Power is fundamental to inter-organizational relationships. As a multidimensional term,
power occurs at various stages of analysis, i.e. organisations, individuals, and relation-
ships, and has different states such as potential power, perceived power, and realized
power.
The literature distinguishes also between structural power (i.e.
dependence)
and behavioral power (i.e. utilization of power), with a few studies considering both at
the same time (Huo et al. 2019, Roseira et al. 2010, Wu & Wu 2015). Opportunism
is frequently associated with the use of power, but it is uncertain if the use of power
is deterrent or opportunistic. Previous research examined power using various lenses,
including power sources, power use in organizations, the disparity between potential
and actual power, and power imbalance or power asymmetry. Studies in the area of
use of power account for a significant proportion of power-related studies in operations
management (Cao & Zhang 2011, Ghadge et al. 2017, Zhao et al. 2018).
The two most appropriate definitions of power in the supply chain literature and
context are found in Dahl (1957) and Sturm & Antonakis (2015). Dahl (1957) defines
power as “the ability of one individual or group to get another unit to do something that
it would not otherwise have done”. Sturm & Antonakis (2015) offer an apt definition of
power as “having the discretion and the means to asymmetrically enforce one’s will over
entities”.
2.3.2
Types of power
The debate on power has constantly revolved around the concept of power in terms of
its roots, bases, and forms (Benton & Maloni 2005, Cowan et al. 2015, Leonidou et al.
2008), often centered on the power-holders (Cox 2001). The terminology has become
increasingly common over time. Widely used concepts include coercive and non-coercive
types of power (Yeung et al. 2009) and exercised and non-exercised sources of power (Cho
et al. 2019, Cowan et al. 2015, He et al. 2013, Leonidou et al. 2008), with power effects
on different outcomes, including conflict (Ampe-N’DA et al. 2020, Arnott et al. 2007,
Gao et al. 2005, Leonidou et al. 2008) and, in particular, satisfaction (Benton & Maloni
2005, Gorton et al. 2015), which are often debated.
==PAGE:37==
2.3 Power
19
The paper by Gölgeci et al. (2018) presents a context for explaining power-based
actions as they develop in supply chain relations. The authors introduce the terms of
dominance, egalitarian, and submissive behaviors and explain the possible impact of
those behaviors on supply chain partners’ relational satisfaction.
Dominance behavior refers to actions taken by a company to force a partner to
behave arbitrarily in the company’s interests (Gölgeci et al. 2018).
Committing to
dominance behavior is a phenomenon when a supply chain partner expects their partner
to comply, often with an implied or explicit suggestion that failure to comply would
have negative consequences. As per Cox (2001), the power status of an organization in
a dyad determines the decision to exercise dominance. Organizations can have various
power advantages, providing them with the ability to use behaviors to exert control
over a partner.
For example, if dependency among partners is imbalanced, the less
dependent company can appear to behave more assertively, assuring in its awareness
that the more dependent partner will likely adhere to expectations. Furthermore, the
less dependent partner may be indifferent regarding the effects of their behavior on the
perception of their relationship by the more dependent partner (Johnsen & Lacoste 2016,
Kumar et al. 1995, 1998). Interestingly, even if there are insufficient power sources to
explain the actions or ensure that the response will be as anticipated, a firm may perform
dominance behaviors (Caniëls et al. 2010).
On the other hand, egalitarian behavior denotes a higher level of reciprocity, equity,
and compromise between the parties, as well as bilateral relationship management (Göl-
geci et al. 2018). While either or both organizations may be able to use dominant forms
of power as such, their intention is to work as equals. The dual concern model acknowl-
edges that self-respect may be followed by concern for the other, resulting in reciprocal
behaviors, including a willingness to negotiate, cooperate and co-manage the relationship,
with equal sharing of relational rents (Johnston et al. 2004). Equity between partners
is achieved by displaying qualities such as reliability and enduring opportunism, with
results that may include establishing trust between partners.
Lastly, Gölgeci et al. (2018) define submissive behavior as compliance with the part-
ner’s criteria. Submissive behaviors are actions that are adaptive, accommodating, and
compliant with the requirements set by a business partner. This generally positions the
compliant partner in a passive partner’s position, using obliging and yielding actions to
establish agreement between the parties. A firm is more likely to adopt a submissive
behavior when it becomes aware that it is more dependent and/or lacks countervailing
power (Caniëls et al. 2010). Gölgeci et al. (2018) identify two primary forms of submis-
sive behavior: willing submissiveness and resentful submissiveness. Willing submissive-
ness is characterized by intrinsic motivation and low to nil resistance to the demanding
partner’s requirements. Conversely, resentful submissiveness is characterized by feeling
obligated to submit while at the same time wanting but feeling incapable of resisting
the partner’s demands.
Based on the source of power, French et al. (1959) identified five power types: expert,
reference, legitimate, reward, and coercive. Later studies further classified them into co-
ercive and non-coercive dimensions (Yeung et al. 2009). Coercive power is characterized
as an overt attempt by one party to exert control over partners through negative ac-
==PAGE:38==
20
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
tions, such as punishments and attempts to withhold support or commitments (Benton
& Maloni 2005, Yeung et al. 2009, Zhaoquan & Yi 2014), whereas non-coercive power
includes the other four power sources and refers to one party’s attempt to encourage
desired behavior in its partners through supportive actions, such as providing assistance
or encouragement (Benton & Maloni 2005, Zhaoquan & Yi 2014). According to French
et al. (1959), the strength of the expert power varies with the degree of knowledge or
perception which the power recipient attributes within a given area to the power holder,
whereas referent power is based on the identification of the power recipient with the
power holder. For example, when a supplier wants to associate with Microsoft on the
basis of admiration and respect, this is referent power.
Legitimate power is defined
as that power stemming from internalized values in the recipient of the power, which
dictates that the holder of the power has a legitimate right to influence the recipient of
the power and that the recipient of the power has an obligation to accept that influence.
Quoting French et al. (1959): “Reward power relies on the power holder’s willingness to
administer positive valences and to suppress or decrease negative valence.” For instance,
when a purchasing company has the power to compensate a supplier with additional
business or favorable care.
In this project, the concepts of coercive and non-coercive power were used to ex-
plain the behavioral dimension of power and investigated the usage of coercive and
non-coercive power by both the buyer and the supplier in a dyadic exchange relation-
ship.
Figure 2.3. The different types of power.
==PAGE:39==
2.3 Power
21
2.3.3
The effects of power in supply chain
Power and dependence were recognized as core elements affecting behaviors in inter-
organizational relationships in the foundational works by French et al. (1959) and Dahl
(1957).
As they accurately stated, a company’s ability to have power over another
mainly relies on a partner’s dependence on it. Power has birthed valuable theories, such
as the resource dependence theory (RDT), which is based on the realisation that the
concept of power is directly associated with control over vital resources (Buvik & Reve
2002). Literature in Supply Chain and Logistics follows this tradition, with a number of
conceptual and empirical projects pertaining to the significance of power and dependence
in portraying the behavior of trading firms (Gölgeci et al. 2018).
As previously mentioned in 2.3.2, supply chain partners are influenced by the effects
of coercive and non-coercive power sources exercised by the partner (Gaski 1984), as well
as by perceptions of the other partner’s power position even if power sources are not
being used (Gaski & Nevin 1985). Consistent with that, Gölgeci et al. (2018) recognize
that dependence plays a pivotal role in the conceptualization of how power-based actions
are chosen by supply chain partners. Dependence can, therefore, be interpreted as the
degree to which one partner needs the other for its business purposes (Kumar et al.
1995, 1998), which is dictated by scarcity, including the number of alternative partners
available with the necessary skills, services, and products.
Power-dependence dynamics have an impact on the actions/responses of supply chain
partners, namely offering rewards and/or enforcing punishments, together with decisions
to accept, oppose or reject partners’ behaviors (Gölgeci et al. 2018, Leonidou et al. 2008,
Terpend & Ashenbaum 2012). As a consequence, the power-dependence positions of the
partners are among the main factors influencing the choice of power-based behaviors
and the response to those behaviors in the supply chain. Generally, relationships must
shift beyond the spot market - where cost is a significant factor and the companies
retain “arm length” ties by having multiple suppliers/customers as near-equivalents for
business needs - in order for power-based conduct to be important to the supply chain
dyad. At the same time, once relations have moved slightly past the spot market and
there is any element of asset specificity, relative power/dependency affects the parties’
dominant, egalitarian, and submissive tendencies.
It is worth noting that power and dependence can change over time between the buyer
and the supplier. For example, as the supply chain literature suggests, a bargaining
position for negotiating in a dyadic relationship may change over time for the better
or the worse (Ghadge et al. 2017, Nair et al. 2011). Relationships between partners
may change from a state of locked-in dependence to a more open commitment (Nair
et al. 2011). Charting the transitions for each partner requires a behavioral vocabulary,
nuanced to reflect both sides of the buyer-supplier relationship.
In the work by Ambrose et al. (2010), the authors employ transaction cost theory
and social exchange theory to compare how buyers and suppliers perceive relationship
mechanisms. According to their study, buyers and suppliers in the same supply chain
relationship (dyadic relationship) have significantly different perceptions of trust, com-
==PAGE:40==
22
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
mitment, resource dependence, power, and relationship success, supporting the findings
of the literature (Corsaro & Snehota 2011, Kumar et al. 1998). For instance, when asked
about satisfaction with the relationship, suppliers typically rate the relationship more
highly than buyers.
The study by Huo et al. (2019) explores how buyer and supplier dependence influ-
ences the other and their own use of coercive and non-coercive power, which contribute
to the two parties’ opportunism, based on data from 240 Chinese companies on their
perceived relations with key suppliers. Their findings illustrate that dependence on the
buyer/supplier is positively linked to the use of coercive and non-coercive power by the
supplier/buyer. The use of coercive power by buyers and suppliers seems to have a
positive impact on their opportunism. Moreover, the use of non-coercive power often
tends to have different effects depending on who is the powerful actor: the use of coer-
cive power by the buyer has a detrimental impact on buyer and supplier opportunism,
whereas the use of power by the supplier does not. Lastly, they find that the use of
coercive and non-coercive power by the buyer has negative effects on its opportunism.
The results of the work by Terpend & Ashenbaum (2012) which examines the in-
tersecting effects of power, trust and supplier network size on various dimensions of
supplier performance indicate that coercive power has a negative association with sup-
plier quality and innovation, referent power has a positive association with all dimensions
of supplier efficiency, and legitimate power has a positive association with supplier pro-
duction, cost, and flexibility. They also argue that increasing network size of suppliers
appears to dampen the impact of coercive and referent power on the performance of
suppliers, while at the same time strengthening this link in the case of legitimate power.
Lastly, they argue that the use of expert power has a negative relationship with the
delivery performance of the supplier, which is aggravated by an increase in the size of
the supplier network.
Overall, literature research findings suggest that coercive forms of power tend to
have a negative effect on supply chain performance, whereas non-coercive forms of power
have a positive effect on performance. These results have been confirmed by the extant
literature by measuring both the direct and indirect effects of coercive and non-coercive
forms of power. For instance, Benton & Maloni (2005) found that coercive forms of power
have a negative effect on trust and commitment while non-coercive forms of power have
a positive effect on trust and commitment. It is possible that when the buyer–supplier
relationship dynamic is characterized by coercion, the trading partner may simply wish
to avoid attention, or at least has no inclination to help his partner beyond meeting
basic performance expectations. Legitimate power, which flows more from a sense of
obligation felt by one’s trading partner, seemed to most influence the performance areas
that might be characterized as the basics of customer service — fast delivery, good price,
and flexibility to volume or mix changes.
==PAGE:41==
2.4 Identified gap in the literature
23
2.4
Identified gap in the literature
To sum up, there is undoubtedly a wealth of literature on power and trust and their
impact on supply chain collaboration and overall performance. And while the analysis
of the literature referred to above has contributed to valuable insights, it has also led to
the detection of gaps in the subject under investigation.
First, the interrelationships between trust, power, supply chain collaboration and
their effect on cooperation and success in the supply chain have generally been indepen-
dently examined (Arnott et al. 2007, Chicksand 2015, Dahwa et al. 2013, Gaski & Nevin
1985, Ghosh & Fedorowicz 2008, Huo et al. 2019, Jain et al. 2014, Lorentz et al. 2011,
Whipple et al. 2010); thus, the knowledge base in this field can be described as somewhat
fragmentary. As both the power and the trust mechanisms do not occur in isolation,
their mutual effect on supply chain collaboration and supply chain performance, as well
as the interplay between them needs to be further clarified. Therefore, additional work
is required, that pools the isolated results in this field and describes the interaction
between them, as well as how these factors affect the collaboration and performance
outcomes of the supply chain.
In addition to untangling the relative influences of trust, power, and SCC on supply
chain performance, more research is needed in the context of dyadic relationships. Most
of the studies focus on either the buyer side (Ghadge et al. 2017, Huo et al. 2019,
Makkonen et al. 2016, Poppo et al. 2016, Wickramanayake & Jayaratne 2016), or the
supplier side (Paparoidamis et al. 2019, Xia et al. 2008, Yeung et al. 2009, Zhaoquan &
Yi 2014), with several studies looking at buyer-supplier relationships (Cho et al. 2019,
Nassar et al. 2019, Roseira et al. 2010, Teller et al. 2016, Wu & Wu 2015) that however
do not belong in the same relationship. There have been a few studies looking at buyer-
supplier dyads (meaning “paired” buyer and supplier companies that are trading partners
in a collaborative or transactional relationship) (Cai et al. 2013, Espadinha-Cruz & Grilo
2019, Mangus et al. 2017). However, in general, these studies used different dyads (i.e.
nor the buyer, neither the supplier firms remained the same). It can, therefore, be argued
that it will be insightful to examine buyer-supplier dyads, where one player remains the
same (case study of the relationships of a buying firm or a supplier firm).
Furthermore, the literature review suggests that more research is needed in the con-
text of Oil and Gas (O&G) supply chains. As Chima (2007) highlights, O&G companies
can benefit more than any other sector from maximizing supply chain efficiencies. In
exploration and production, the types of shipments made vary widely from gloves to
cranes and cement to name a few, and much of the work and tasks are routine with
most of the large and critical operations being planned in advance (Chima 2007). How-
ever, according to this literature review sample, the author was able to find only a few
studies focusing on how supply chain collaboration and integration can be used by an
O&G company to improve operational efficiency (Chima 2007, Ebrahimi 2015, Hoffman
et al. 2004). It, therefore, seemed appropriate to concentrate the emphasis of this study
in the oil and gas sector.
==PAGE:42==
24
Literature Review & Conceptual Development
2.5
Conceptual Framework
After carefully reviewing the relevant literature, different variables describing the con-
cepts of trust, power, and supply chain collaboration were determined. These variables,
were then further grouped depending on similarities/differences in their constructs and
their results on trust, power and SCC respectively. The determined variables and their
groups are presented in Figure 2.4. Then, the relationships between these variables and
the key concepts were identified, as well as the relationships between the key concepts
and their effects on Supply Chain Performance. The identified relationships are also pre-
sented in Figure 2.4, and were explored throughout the course of this research project
and the analysis of the data.
As previously discussed in 2.2.3, it was deduced that trust can be split into three
categories, namely normative-affective trust, cognitive-based trust, and calculative trust.
The variables of normative-affective trust are closeness, openness, security, and credibil-
ity. In the same fashion, the variables of cognitive-based trust are integrity, reliability
and competence, while calculative trust can be described by reward/penalties schemes
and dependence. These variables were used in order to determine the (dominant) type
of trust in the studied relationships.
Similarly, power can be split into two categories: exercised and unexercised power.
This distinction is deemed very important, as according to the literature, it can have
majorly different results on the effects of power in the relationship. Hence, exercised
power can further be split into two variables, namely coercive and non-coercive power,
while unexercised power will be described by the perception of power from the trading
partners in the relationship. These variables were used to determine the type of power
that is present in the studied relationships.
As far as Supply Chain Collaboration (SCC) is concerned, the variables that consti-
tute SCC have been identified and analysed in 2.1.3. It is assumed that all of the seven
components/variables of SCC should be present in a successful collaborative relation-
ship, hence this research attempted to investigate their contribution to SCC (how many
of these were actually utilized and in which ways).
The concepts that were investigated, and, therefore, the subsequent research ques-
tions of this project were:
1. How does power affect supply chain collaboration?
2. How does trust affect supply chain collaboration?
3. How does trust affect the use/perception of power in a business relationship?
4. How does power affect the presence/type of trust developed in a business relation-
ship?
5. Which components of SCC are present in collaborative supply chains and how do
they affect the overall collaboration?

The conceptual framework that was developed, was consequently “explored” using a
case company as the buying firm, and examining dyadic relationships with some of their
most strategic suppliers. This was done through interviews with Category and Contract
Managers from the buying firm, as well as Key Account Managers from the supplier
companies. The exact research process and analysis is described in more detail in the
chapters that follow.
==PAGE:44==
26
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 3
Research Approach
After carrying out an extensive literature review of relevant academic sources, and de-
veloping the conceptual framework, a case company was chosen as an industry example
to explore the variables and aspects of this framework that were actually present in
the studied collaborative relationships, in connection with the research questions of this
study. In this chapter, the methodology that was followed regarding the data collec-
tion, and the data analysis of this project is described. First, a brief description of the
case company used for this study is presented, followed by a detailed explanation of the
data collection method. Finally, the last section of this chapter includes a thorough
description of the data analysis method. In Figure 3.1 an illustration of the complete
methodology process is depicted.
3.1
Case study
In order to answer the research questions set out at the beginning of this project, five
dyadic buyer–supplier relationships (i.e. paired buyers and suppliers that are involved
in a business relationship), drawn from the Oil & Gas industry, were examined. The oil
and gas sector was chosen, as it is a theoretically relevant context for studying power
in relationships (Chima 2007). Very few sectors can profit more than the oil and gas
companies from optimizing the supply chain efficiencies through effective supplier re-
lationship management, since these companies are highly dependent on suppliers to
provide complex services and critical equipment to support ongoing projects and opera-
tions (Chen et al. 2011, Chima 2007). Moreover, the fact that the author was employed
at the case company as a student assistant in Supply Chain Project Management team,
simplified the processes, since communication was easier and data/resource availability
was increased.
The case company (buying firm) is a drilling contractor and one of the market leaders
in the Oil and Gas industry, with a reported revenue of 279 million USD and a financial
uptime of 97.5% in the first quarter of 2020. Having more than 4000 active suppliers,
and being involved in more than 30 collaborative relationships with their strategic and
operational suppliers, this company was a very interesting case to investigate the effects
of power and trust in collaborative buyer-supplier relationships. To identify the specific
relationships that would be investigated, the following criteria were used:
• The supplier should be of strategic importance to the buying firm.

• There should be some form of established relationship management with the sup-

dependence) where collaboration initiatives are not as successful).
• The supplier companies should belong in different categories or competing in the
same category, based on the products/services they offer to the buying firm.
• The companies would be willing to be completely open and transparent with the
researcher, in order to ensure that full and reliable data collection would be possi-

3.2
Data Collection Method
When faced with the dilemma of choosing the best and most appropriate method for
collecting the qualitative data necessary, two methods were considered. The first one
was to send out questionnaires, and the second was to conduct face-to-face or phone
interviews.
==PAGE:48==
30
Research Approach
Questionnaires can either be delivered online or paper-based, and consist of sets
of questions which all respondents are required to complete. Once a questionnaire is
developed, it can be handed out with little effort to a large number of people (Akbayrak
2000, Phellas et al. 2011). However, this does not guarantee a high response rate, as it
is very often the case that people do not concern themselves with responding properly
or at all to these questionnaires. In addition, questionnaires imply a large volume of
data that needs to be coded, analysed and evaluated in the case of a large number of
responses (Akbayrak 2000, Phellas et al. 2011).
Interviews, on the other hand, are usually conducted on a one-on-one basis (Alshen-
qeeti 2014, Gill et al. 2008). They require a large amount of time for the researcher during
the interviews and also for transcribing and coding the data. Another way of conducting
interviews is through “focus groups” typically consisting of one researcher and several
participants in a single session (Gill et al. 2008). In interviews and focus groups, the
researcher has better control over the response rate and the quality/validity of the data.
Moreover, the researcher has a better understanding of whether specific questions make
the interview subjects nervous or whether the subjects struggle to answer the question
where the interviewer may clarify questions not understood by the respondent and may
even ask for further elaboration, as well as the use of open questions, which allows for
more detailed answers and enables the researcher to guide the interview in the desired
direction (Akbayrak 2000, Gill et al. 2008, Phellas et al. 2011). Last but not least, in
general, respondents experience questions being asked by a sympathetic listener as more
rewarding than the chore of filling in a form for some anonymous researcher.
Taking all of the above into account, it was decided to continue with conducting
one-on-one interviews and focus groups, instead of distributing questionnaires.
This
particular method was considered the most appropriate considering the nature of the
research, being an exploratory case study, and the amount of data required. This way,
the researcher would be able to gain valuable insights into the key stakeholders’ distinct
perspectives on the effects of power and trust in collaboration and the most important
aspects of SCC in the relationships being studied. Furthermore, a deeper understanding
of the challenges faced in these relationships was made possible through the collection of
the interviewees’ subjective points of view. In the following sections, the exact procedure
for the preparation of interview questions and the set-up of interviews is described in
detail.
3.2.1
Formulating the interview questions
A list of questions presented in Table 3.1 was formulated using the conceptual framework
provided in Figure 2.4, which was introduced in section 2.5, taking into account the
literature review and the research questions underlying the scope of this project. The
questions were divided into five categories/groups, representing the five relationships-
arrows in the conceptual framework (see Figure 3.3). These questions were then used
during the interviews with the buying firm and its suppliers. This way, the interview
==PAGE:49==
3.2 Data Collection Method
31
questions were directly related to the aspects of the collaborative relationships that are
of research interest.
Table 3.1. Interview questions
G1: Power on SCC
1. How would you describe the power asymmetry in your relationship with company X?
Who is the strongest trading partner and how is this power exercised to the weakest counterpart?
2. In which ways would you say that this power has affected your collaboration with company X?
Is it an overall positive outcome or negative?
G2: Trust on SCC
3. How would you describe the trust in your relationship with company X?
Is it more based on dependence and rewards/penalties, on integrity/reliability and competence
or on closeness/openness and credibility?
4. In which ways would you say that trust has affected your collaboration with company X?
Is it an overall positive outcome or negative?
G3: Trust on Power
5. How would you interpret the effect of trust has affected the power asymmetry/power
exercise in your relationship with company X?
G4: Power on Trust
6. How would you interpret the effect of (coercive/non-coercive/perceived) power
has affected trust in your relationship with company X?
G5: SCC & SCP
7. How frequently do you communicate with the supplier/buyer?
(collaborative communication)
8. To what degree do you share sensitive and/or supply chain-related information with the
strategic partner (information sharing)? Do you also share resources? (resource sharing)
9. To what extent do you orchestrate decisions in supply chain planning and operations in
order to optimize the supply chain benefits (decision synchronization)?
10. Would you say that you have developed a better understanding of and response to
the market and competitive environment by working together with company X?
(joint knowledge creation)
11. How would you interpret this collaboration with company X has affected the supply chain
performance? Is it an overall positive outcome or negative?
As can be seen by Table 3.1, the five question categories are the effect of power on
supply chain collaboration, the effect of trust on supply chain collaboration, the effect
of trust on power in the collaborative relationship and the other way around (the effect
of power on trust), and lastly the effect of supply chain collaboration on the overall
supply chain performance. The questions were approved by experienced professors and
academics before they were finalized.
