3.2.2
Interview Process
In order to gather as reliable and accurate empirical data as possible, a total of 10 in-
terviews were conducted. The initial goal was to interview a Category Manager and a
Contract Manager from the buying firm for each dyadic relationship and the Key Ac-
count Manager or Sales Representative managing the relationship from each supplier
company (total of 5 interviews with representatives from the supplier companies). Sev-
eral restrictions and unprecedented events following the COVID-19 pandemic and the
historically low oil prices that shook the oil and gas industry, did not allow for that goal
to be achieved in the tight available time-frame. More specifically, while all interviews
were conducted with the managers of the buying firm (half of them in the form of focus
groups, and half of them in one-on-one interviews), only 3 of 5 of the suppliers’ represen-
tatives were reached and interviewed (one-on-one interviews). This poses a significant
limitation in the research, one which is well acknowledged and taken into account in the
validity of the findings that are presented in later chapters.
Most of the respondents were based in Europe (two-thirds) with the remaining third
situated in North America (mainly at European companies’ regional offices). This also
offered a clear insight into both headquarters and subsidiaries’ opinions. As previously
mentioned, the respondents were senior managers (ca.
11 years of management ex-
perience on average) highly involved in managing the exchange relationship, holding
roles such as Category Manager or Contract Manager (buying firm) and Key Account
Managers (supplier companies). The interview questions were sent in advance to all
respondents, enabling fuller answers to be obtained. As part of the interview preamble,
==PAGE:51==
3.3 Data Analysis Method
33
the respondents were asked to keep in mind only the specific dyadic relationship which
was investigated. All the interviews were carried out using a widely known online confer-
encing platform and all respondents were asked if they agreed to be recorded during the
sessions to maximize the transcription quality, with the understanding that confidential-
ity would be observed. This maximised openness and objectivity. The interviews were
semi-structured, following the interview questions of Table 3.1, but with flexible and in-
formal dialogue that provided the opportunity for the interviewer to obtain an in-depth
understanding of the opinions, perceptions, and expectations of the respondents towards
the effect of trust and power between buyers and suppliers on supply chain collaboration
and supply chain performance. Each of the interviews had a duration between 40 and
50 minutes. All interviews were conducted within a period of seven weeks.
3.3
Data Analysis Method
In order to analyze the interview data, an adaptation of the Thematic Qualitative
Analysis process described in Burnard (1991) was used. More specifically, the exact
steps that were followed are described below:
• Step 1: Familiarization with the interview data.
Transcripts were read through, and notes on general themes within transcripts
were made throughout the reading. These first notes reflected the first impressions
one would get by reading the transcripts.
• Step 2: Carefully reviewing the transcripts.
In this step, the goal was to be immersed in the interview data.
Hence, the
transcripts were read again, one by one and line-by-line very carefully, trying to
become more fully aware of the “frame of reference” of the respondent.
• Step 3: Open coding.
Transcripts were read again, and as many headings as needed were written down to
describe all dimensions of the content. At this point headings were freely created.
Some of the criteria used for coding were if something was repeated in several
places, if the respondent explicitly stated that something was of great importance,
if something similar was stated in the literature or reminded of a theory/concept
that was studied during the literature review, or simply if the researcher considered
something relevant and important.
• Step 4: Categorizing the coded data.
In this step, the list of headings was surveyed and grouped under higher-order
categories in order to reduce the number of headings by integrating some of the
similar ones into broader categories. The work in this step was more general and
abstract, as the attempt was to further conceptualise the data.
==PAGE:52==
34
Research Approach
• Step 5: Re-coding each transcript.
Then, each transcript was “coded” according to the list of categories and sub-
categories. This was achieved by collating the transcripts’ text into spreadsheets
and labeling the data, using “color-coding” too. Adjustments were made where
deemed necessary, to ensure that the vast majority of data was covered.
• Step 6: Connecting the categories.
The transcripts were reread alongside the categories and sub-categories that were
identified in the previous steps. The goal was to identify the connections between
the categories and the sub-categories and to make sure that all the data was covered
with this structure, as these categories and connections would be the main findings
of the study.
• Step 7: Side-by-side comparing buyer vs. supplier interviews.
Lastly, the transcripts were also compared per relationship studied, to exemplify
similarities and differences between the perspectives of the respondents on the
same questions or categories.
This process was followed and implemented in the each interview transcript and the
resulted coded transcripts can be found in Appendix C. This method for analyzing the
interview data can be categorized as “inductive”, as although the questions were directly
connected to the conceptual framework, the data analysis did not aim at testing this
framework or its validity, but rather at exploring the variables and aspects that actually
apply in the studied relationships and narrowing the field.
==PAGE:53==
CHAPTER 4
Findings
This chapter presents the findings of the analysis of the interview data that were drawn
specifically to the case study of the five dyadic buyer-supplier relationships, and more
specifically the common insights of the buying firm regarding the effect of trust on SCC;
the effect of power on SCC; the effect of power on trust and vice versa and the present
collaborative initiatives of the studied relationships. Then, a dyadic comparison is also
presented of the three dyadic buyer-supplier relationships that the author was able to
interview both the buyer and the supplier, to exemplify the most prominent differences
and similarities in their perceptions. Some important quotes from the interviews are
also included in this chapter, while the interested reader is prompted to Appendix B
where the relevant quotes are summarized in the form of tables. Lastly, the complete
set of transcripts can be found in Appendix C.
4.1
General Perceptions of the Buyer versus
the Supplier Companies
In this section, the general perceptions of the buying firm representatives are presented,
together with a comparison of the respective views of the supplier companies’ represen-
tatives. The section is divided in six sub-sections, corresponding to the six higher-order
categories that were identified during the analysis of the interview data, namely (i) the
effect of power on SCC, (ii) the effect of trust on SCC, (iii), the effect of trust on power
asymmetry, (iv) the effect of power on trust, (v) aspects of SCC present in the studied
relationships, and (vi) the overall effect of SCC on the studied relationships.
4.1.1
The effect of Power on Supply Chain
Collaboration
Regarding the effect of power on SCC, the analysis of the interview data revealed three
key dimensions: (i) the perception on the most powerful trading partner, (ii) the per-
ception and exercise of power in the relationship by the trading partners, and (iii) the
assessment of the overall impact of power on SCC and the business relationship. These
three dimensions are further analysed below.
==PAGE:54==
36
Findings
Most powerful trading partner
As far as the perception on which company is the most “powerful” in the relationship
and has the most leverage, in 4 out of the 5 studied relationships, the buying firm con-
siders the supplier to be most powerful trading partner, as opposed to only 1 in 5 where
they consider that they are in a better position than the supplier. The main reasons
they mention are the size of the organisations, the level of dependency and the current
market conditions (oil prices, COVID-19 etc.).
“
They (SUP5) have much more power over us than we have over them,
and they can certainly dictate things: they can affect the uptime and
the performance of our rig. So the power sits in their hands.
– Category Manager for SUP5
”
On the other hand, the supplier companies seem to agree that the buying firm has
more leverage in the relationship, the reason being that the buying firm is the ultimate
decision maker with regard to whether to collaborate with them or choose one of their
competitors.
“
...When it comes to the power symmetry in the relationship, ulti-
mately (and I don’t feel like it’s pushed down our throat), BF has
the most power in the relationship, […] because they make the final
commercial decision.
– Key Account Manager for SUP1
”
More insights into the other quotes from the interviews are available in Table B.1.
Perception and exercise of power
Regarding the perception and exercise of power in the studied relationships, the respon-
dents from the buying firm seem to agree that the suppliers make sure that the buying
firm knows who holds the most power in the business relationship, even when they are
not making “explicit” use of it. They share some examples of incidents where the suppli-
ers (who they perceive to be more powerful than them) exercised their power on them,
such as in negotiations or other business deals to achieve the most profitable results
for their company, regardless of what the effect would be in the collaboration and the
overall business relationship. However, they also highlight that the use of power varies
==PAGE:55==
4.1 General Perceptions of the Buyer versus the Supplier Companies
37
substantially from case to case and that the power-holders make vigilant/strategic use
of their respective power, since the overall market conditions are very dynamic and even
cyclical, so they acknowledge that they might not stay for long in this favourable po-
sition. In the one case where the buying firm considers themselves as more powerful
than the supplier, they perceive that they do not make use of their power/leverage in
order to achieve more favorable outcomes for their organisation (e.g. strategic sourcing,
negotiating lower prices etc.).
“
...on general terms whether they use their power or not, that varies
a lot from case to case.
[…] (In negotiations) I feel that SUP2 is
more confident and using more of their market position, and what
they are providing to us to their benefit. I am not saying that I have
experienced any kind of mistrust, or misuse of power.
Not yet at
least. […] It’s all fine at the beginning, but then at some point, this
power kicks in.
– Category Manager for SUP2
”
The suppliers’ views on this (who consider the buying firm as being the most powerful
trading partner in the relationship) are very similar to those of the buying firm, noting
that the buying firm utilises their power strategically, usually when capital expenditures
are concerned or big investment projects (see Table B.2). They also mention that the
buying firm utilizes their power by making them work harder to earn their business so
that the suppliers understand who is the most powerful partner. It is also mentioned in
one of the interviews with the Global Key Account Manager for SUP2, that even though
the buying firm consider themselves as a collaborative and partnering organisation, when
it comes to business transactions and agreements, they are usually very straightforward
and they use their power by offering ultimatums. One of the examples shared by the Key
Account Manager for SUP1, is that the buying firm has “threatened” to recertify some
of the supplier’s equipment as their own to reduce their dependency on them. He also
highlights that although SUP1 understand the high level of dependency of the buying
firm for their equipment/services, they consider that they never took advantage of that.
“
What bothers me […] is that they talk partnership all day long, but
at the end of the day […] when they want something, it’s a one way
conversation. You do this or don’t, your choice. No discussion. You
either accept it or you don’t accept it.
– Global Key Account Manager for SUP2
”
==PAGE:56==
38
Findings
Overall effect of power on SCC
From the buyer’s perspective, the overall outcome of use of power is negative in any
relationship. In certain examples that were shared during the interview with the Senior
Partnering Manager for SUP1 from the buying firm (see Table B.3 or Appendix C), it
was highlighted several times that when the supplier did not live up to their promises
and they used their market position and power advantage towards the buying firm, the
trust was broken down at a very high level and even though SUP1 make efforts to gain
the trust back, it is emphasized that it will take a lot of time to rebuild the trust. The
respondent stresses that this use of power from SUP1 left a mark in the relationship
and negatively affected the collaboration thereafter. The Category Manager for SUP5
mentions that although they could “bypass” this supplier in order to reduce their total
cost of ownership, that would have negative effects on the collaboration with SUP5 in
the longer term, hence the buying firm choose not to use their power in the relationship,
but instead they try to increase the collaboration in the hopes of reducing the exertion
of power from SUP5. Finally, the Category Manager for SUP2 makes an interesting re-
mark that both partners (buyer and supplier) should leave their power baggage outside
the relationship in order to ensure close collaboration and partnership.
“
That (misuse of power) leaves a footprint or a fingerprint on the
relationship long term. They’re just hard things to forget […] I think
there’s still pockets within the organization that still reflect back on
that mistrust and I think that’s the negative outcome that there is
still a little bit of a stress.
– Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1
”
“
...you could say that the use of our power was at least perceived
as coercive, in order to get work done.
In fact, we did.
It took
about a year, […] but what is funny is that the company did come
forward, and we ended up having an open collaboration. […] Unfor-
tunately, we lost all the business after the fact that we used our power.
– Global Key Account Manager for SUP2
”
In general, the perspective of the suppliers is in agreement with that of the buying
firm, as they also point out that there can be no genuine collaboration if the partners
exercise their respective powers on each other. An interesting finding emerged from an
example that was shared by the Global Key Account Manager for SUP2, in which the
==PAGE:57==
4.1 General Perceptions of the Buyer versus the Supplier Companies
39
use of power was found to momentarily improve the business performance, but with
adverse effects on the long-term benefits of the collaboration, as the trust was “broken”
and the relationship became purely transactional thereafter.
4.1.2
The effect of Trust on Supply Chain Collaboration
Regarding the effect of trust on SCC, the analysis of the interview data revealed two
key dimensions: (i) the trading parters’ perception on the type of trust present in the
relationship, (ii) the assessment of the overall impact of trust on SCC and the business
relationship. These dimensions are further analysed below.
Dominant type of trust in the studied relationships
When asked about the type of trust in the studied relationships, the respondents from
the buying firm highlight the high dependence on some of these suppliers (e.g. SUP1,
SUP5), as well as the reliability and competencies of their partners (e.g. SUP2, SUP3).
Regarding the use of rewards or penalties and the effect these have on building and
maintaining the trust between the partners, the general finding is that although there
might be some penalties (e.g. liquidated damages or interest for late payments) or re-
wards (e.g. retroactive annual bonuses) in place, these are used very rarely or not at all,
and therefore they do not have an impact on trust. Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1
points out that this can also be seen as testament of the trust in the relationship, as re-
sorting to utilising rewards or penalties would make the relationship purely transactional.
“
...we are completely dependent upon them, but they are also generally
quite reliable. I think they are a quite open company to […] collabo-
rating and sharing in how we work together.
– Category Manager for SUP5
”
On the other hand, the suppliers mention that closeness, openness and credibility
are the most important constructs of trust that are currently present in the studied
relationships. The Key Account Manager for SUP3 mentions that although there have
been projects where there were rewards, these were purely performance-based and they
did not affect the level of trust in the relationship (see Table B.4).
An interesting
remark is made by the Global Key Account Manager for SUP2, who mentions that
effective collaboration requires credibility and trust to be built and maintained in the
relationship.
Another important finding emerging from the responses of the respondents is that
the level of trust varies significantly in the different hierarchy levels within the organisa-
tions. For example, both the Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1 from the buying firm
==PAGE:58==
40
Findings
and the Key Account Manager for SUP1 from the supplier SUP1 mention that there is
more trust in the lower hierarchy levels (operational and tactical levels) and much less
at the higher levels (strategic and senior leadership levels).
“
I think the last one. I get some more based closeness openness and
credibility. […] There’s more to it […] The fact that we’re “married”,
and they got a ton of our equipment, it forces us into our relationship.
And I think that at the lower levels, there’s a good amount of trust. I
think at the senior leadership levels, at times, there’s been a little bit
of mistrust.
– Key Account Manager for SUP1
”
Overall effect of trust on SCC in the studied relationships
Regarding their overall perception of trust in the relationship and if they evaluate its
impact as positive or negative, the respondents from the buying firm shared some in-
teresting insights. For example, the Senior Negotiator & Contract Manager for SUP1
mentioned that the buying firm shows some kind of “false trust”, where on the surface
it looks like there is trust in the relationship with their partners, but deep down that
trust is not really there. He also said that they have a difficulty to evaluate “the things
where you can’t put a number to” and that they tend to rely a lot on past experiences.
This view was also supported by the Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1, who men-
tioned that the buying firm does not give “much leeway” to their partners, as well as
the Category Manager for SUP2 who doubted the overall existence of trust in the rela-
tionship with SUP2. Moreover, the buying firm representatives generally seemed more
reluctant to evaluate positively the impact of trust in the studied business relationships,
evaluating it as “rather negative”, “maybe neutral”, “maybe not negatively”.
“
I don’t think the trust has never been there in this relationship,
unfortunately.
– Category Manager for SUP2
”
Conversely, the representatives of the supplier companies seemed to have a more
positive view on the impact of trust in the studied relationships, evaluating it as “100%
positive”, “definitely […] positive”, “always been positive”. Furthermore, both the Key
Account Manager for SUP1 and the Key Account Manager for SUP3 agreed that the
existence of trust in the respective relationships has never made them too complacent,
==PAGE:59==
4.1 General Perceptions of the Buyer versus the Supplier Companies
41
highlighting that despite the trust, they always get monitored by the buying firm. Fi-
nally, the Global Key Account Manager exemplified the importance of trust and being
proactive and transparent in effective and successful collaborative supply chains.
“
It’s 100% positive (the impact of trust on SCC). I know it can become
complacent for sure […] but if something feels out of place, we get
questioned on it and if we start charging something that’s stupid,
it’s a red flag it’s easily caught if we’re trying to be complacent than
gouge BF.
– Key Account Manager for SUP1
”
Another interesting finding arose from the answers of both the supplier companies
and the buying firm, and it is regarding the consistency of the people managing the
business relationships. More specifically, it was emphasized by both the Senior Part-
nering Manager for SUP1 and the Key Account Manager for SUP3, that the buying
firm is a “dynamic organisation”, where people change positions very often, especially
in Procurement. According to their responses, this instability in the people managing
the relationships has always been a challenge for the buying firm, as there is “just a
handful of people that have maintained the continuity between the companies”. This is
an interesting finding, especially considering the fact that it is people who build and
maintain the relationships between organisations. Table B.5 in Appendix B summarizes
all the relevant quotes from the interviews.
“
We have a dynamic organization. […] (There are) just a handful of
people that have maintained the continuity between the two companies.
[…] that is hugely important in defining all of BF’s relationships, and
where some of the pitfalls are.
– Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1
”
4.1.3
The effect of trust on power asymmetry in the
studied relationships
When asked about the effect of trust on power asymmetry, the answers retrieved from
the buying firm respondents seem to vary depending on the individual relationship.
Some contended that signals of trust have aided in limiting the exercise of power in
==PAGE:60==
42
Findings
the relationships, while others mentioned that it had no significant effect on the power
asymmetry.
More specifically, according to the Category Manager for SUP2, if there was more
trust between the two partners (BF and SUP2), that would have led to less exercise
of power from both sides, which is in agreement with the views of Senior Negotiator &
Contract Manager for SUP1 who mentioned that the lack of trust adds to the power
dynamics in the relationship. The Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1 also disclosed
that SUP1 have been using their power more strategically due to the trust and commit-
ment that has been exhibited by the buying firm, while the Category Manager for SUP5
observed that the supplier has become more flexible and open due to the commitment
and trust that the buying firm showed towards them, thus limiting the impact of power
asymmetry.
“
...sometimes there’s a lack of trust and that adds to the power
dynamics. […] Then that lack of trust has probably resulted in both
parties not getting what they optimally could have gotten from the
relationship, the negotiation, whatever you want to look at it as.
– Senior Negotiator & Contract Manager for SUP1
”
On the other hand, the Category Manager for SUP3 pointed out that despite the
established long-standing trusted relationship between the two partners (BF and SUP3),
this has not limited the use of power from SUP3 now that they are in a more favorable
market - and therefore power - position. In addition, the Category Manager for SUP4
divulged that possibly the only reason for not utilizing any leverage the buying firm has
over SUP4 is due to the potential partnering benefits, and not because of the existence
of trust.
As far as the views of the suppliers are concerned, there appears to be a consensus
towards the notion that trust can scale down the exercise of power from the business
partners involved in the collaboration. For instance, the Key Account Manager for SUP1
stated that if there is a challenging issue that can negatively impact the partnership, if
this partnership is built on trust, then the partners will be able to overcome these difficul-
ties and come up with better solutions. The Global Key Account Manager for SUP2 gave
another insightful perspective, using an example where the buyer used coercive power
towards them and illustrated his point that being trustful, responsive, and transparent
can minimize the negative effects of the exercise of power in a business relationship. A
summary of the quotes relating to the impact of trust on power asymmetry can be found
in Table B.6.
==PAGE:61==
4.1 General Perceptions of the Buyer versus the Supplier Companies
43
4.1.4
The effect of power on trust in the studied
relationships
Regarding the impact that power exercise has on trust in the studied collaborative rela-
tionships, the general consensus among the buying firm and their suppliers seems to be
that use of coercive power “breaks” the trust in the relationship, leaving a long-lasting
negative imprint that is difficult to be restored. The Senior Partnering Manager for
SUP1 shared an insightful example, explaining that when SUP1 took advantage of their
auspicious market position and used coercive power towards the buying firm, that inci-
dent broke the trust between the two firms and left a “fingerprint” in the relationship.
That led to having “pockets of trust” and “pockets of mistrust” between the two compa-
nies even after many years of working together. The Category Manager for SUP3 also
expressed the same opinion when she was asked the same question about the current
situation with SUP3 taking advantage of their power. Another intriguing example was
shared by the Category Manager for SUP2, in which she emphasized that when partners
resort to using their respective power in the relationship, that is a result of losing the
trust in the collaboration and each other.
“
...then that (misusing your power) leaves a footprint or a fingerprint
on the relationship long term. […] And so, ultimately what it does -
and it’s very clear - is it breaks down trust. And so that’s probably
where we’re at with SUP1. The trust […] WAS broken down at a very
high level. […] And I would say we have pockets of trust and pockets
of distrust still from a long legacy of engagement.
– Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1
”
The suppliers seem to agree with these views, with the Global Key Account Manager
for SUP2 sharing a very illustrative example of the effects of power on trust and SCC
overall (see Table B.7 or Appendix C). In the example, the supplier company (SUP2)
used coercive power in a business relationship to ensure the money they were owed would
be paid. The consequence of this was that they obtained the desired outcome, but their
use of coercive power also resulted in a termination of the contractual partnership once
the responsibilities of the partners had been met. The Key Account Manager for SUP3
had a more conservative view on this, and accentuated that if the trust foundation is
solid enough, moderate use of power in the relationship cannot have a long-term negative
outcome.
==PAGE:62==
44
Findings
4.1.5
Aspects of Supply Chain Collaboration present in
the studied relationships
As far as the specific aspects of SCC that are present in the studied relationships, the
analysis of the interview data revealed that (i) collaborative communication, (ii) in-
formation sharing, (iii) resources sharing, (iv) decision synchronization, and (v) joint
knowledge creation were present on some level in each of the studied relationships. A
more detailed presentation of these findings is available in this section.
Collaborative communication
One of the aspects of supply chain collaboration that has been present in all the studied
relationships was collaborative communication. As indicated by the relevant quotes in
Table B.8, the different levels inside the organisations utilize different means and have
different frequency of communication. More specifically, it was widely mentioned by
both the buying firm respondents and the suppliers that communication at the oper-
ational level is the most frequent (multiple times per week), utilizing emails or phone
calls and exchanging transactional information and updates. The tactical levels (Catego-
ry/Contract Managers and Key Account Managers) are said to have regular structured
calls (for example biweekly or monthly) to discuss current challenges, whereas at the
strategic levels, the communication is even more structured, with Quarterly Business
Reviews (QBR) or biannual meetings, where the senior leadership members meet to
discuss supply chain performance, escalated issues, project pipeline and current open
issues.
However, as highlighted by the Senior Negotiator and Contract Manager for SUP1,
the Category Manager for SUP3 and the Key Account Manager for SUP3, reality is usu-
ally different than what was planned, with QBRs for example not happening as often
as they should. Each of them deemed that the aspect of collaborative communication
would be a potential area of improvement for the buying firm.
“
So, the more strategic levels, we are supposed to meet and discuss
more critical issues and performance issues in our Quarterly Business
Review meetings (QBRs), but they just never get organized, because
there is always something happening. Personally, I talk with them
once a month to discuss some ongoing issues and projects.
– Category Manager for SUP3
”
==PAGE:63==
4.1 General Perceptions of the Buyer versus the Supplier Companies
45
“
Communication is the number one key […] but especially when
it comes to supply chain collaboration, if you don’t have timely,
effective, proactive communication, you’re never going to build trust
or credibility with anybody.
[…] Without having collaborative com-
munication both with your clients, your suppliers, your contractors,
you’re going to fail. Your supply chain you’ll never, never perform.
– Global Key Account Manager for SUP2
”
A couple other important findings from the supplier side may also be identified when
analysing the interview transcripts. For instance, the Key Account Manager for SUP3
mentioned that in the past the collaborative communication with the buying firm was
better organized, more structured, more formalized and more open, pointing out to
the lack of proper planning and organizing. He also acknowledged the impact of the
current uncertain business environment on SCC, which, according to him, has led many
people to change positions or become redundant, disturbing the balance and continuity
of communication between supply chain partners. The Global Key Account Manager for
SUP2 also emphasized that collaborative communication and openness are the number
one key components of collaborative supply chains, and that without effective, timely
and proactive communication between all the supply chain partners, their supply chains
will never deliver high performance.
Information Sharing
Regarding the information sharing aspect of SCC, the respondents from the buying firm
seem to agree that the buying firm is generally open in sharing any kind of information
necessary to their suppliers, apart from commercially sensitive information that could
be subject to legal compliance and antitrust issues. For example, the Senior Partnering
Manager for SUP1 mentioned that they “talk as openly as the law allows”. Furthermore,
they generally consider the buying firm as being more open in information sharing than
the suppliers in the studied relationships (see the quotes from the Category Manager for
SUP1, Category Manager for SUP2, Contract Manager for SUP4 in Table B.9). The
types of information shared by the buying firm with their suppliers are performance re-
lated (equipment uptime/downtime, benchmarking against competitors of the suppliers),
technical and maintenance related, spend numbers and project pipeline, and it was also
mentioned that they are open in sharing forecasts and even more sensitive information
in order to be more engaging, open and proactive in the collaboration.
On the other hand, the suppliers representatives mentioned that they are very open in
sharing information with the buying firm, even market-sensitive information or sharing
their knowledge about the buying firm’s competitors way of working. The Key Account
Manager for SUP3 said that: “we also share information on how other companies are
==PAGE:64==
46
Findings
doing things” and the Key Account Manager for SUP1 seemed to agree with this, say-
ing that “sharing of information that shouldn’t be shared is part of the deal”. Finally,
the Global Key Account Manager for SUP2 shared some interesting insights about the
importance and the value of information sharing, characterizing it as “key” in building
stronger relationships and helping both companies overcome the forthcoming challenges
by allowing them to be prepared and make informed decisions. All the relevant quotes
from the interviews are summarized in Table B.9.
Resources Sharing
As far as sharing of resources is concerned, this aspect seems to be treated quite differ-
ently depending on the specific studied relationship. With the biggest and more powerful
OEM & SPP suppliers (SUP1 and SUP5), it was mentioned that the trading partners
have had some projects in the past which involved sharing of resources, or investing
together in joint responsibility projects. On the other hand, with the 3PL provider and
the Manpower Service Provider (SUP2 and SUP3 respectively), the joint resources as-
pect involves collaborative teams with personnel of both companies being allocated to
the same project or use of consultants (see Table B.10 for the specific relevant quotes as
they were obtained from the interviews) to achieve mutually beneficial results.
Decision Synchronisation
Both the buying firm representatives and the suppliers commonly cited that the aspect
of decision synchronisation is not at the desired levels from both sides of the partnership.
More specifically, the buying firm commented that they try to share their project plans
in advance with their partners to help them plan their respective operations accordingly,
as well as by engaging people from different departments on the organisations in these
plans to ensure cross-departmental agreement.
“
...sometimes we would consult them much in advance. Sometimes,
probably not.
– Contract Manager for SUP4
”
On the other side, some of the suppliers mentioned utilizing B2B (Business-to-
Business) solutions to simplify some common processes (e.g. order processing) or being
involved in consultation processes, but the main finding was their almost unanimous
request for proper forecasts from the buying firm to help them plan and schedule their
operations. The Senior Negotiator & Contract Manager for SUP1 also recognises this
need for the buying firm to become better at providing reliable forecasts to their sup-
==PAGE:65==
4.1 General Perceptions of the Buyer versus the Supplier Companies
47
pliers. The specific quotes regarding decision synchronisation can be found in Table B.11.
“
...we would love it if BF were able to provide us with some forecasts
or any indications about their future projects. They did that in the
past […] so we always kind of knew what was going on the next half
year. That we don’t see any longer, and it’s a shame because it was
a great help […] also because we might have some input for it. […]
So, if we can just look half year into the future, that would be brilliant.
– Key Account Manager for SUP3
”
Joint Knowledge Creation
Different views were provided regarding the joint knowledge creation aspect of SCC in
the studied relationships, varying not only among the suppliers but also within the buy-
ing firm. Firstly, some of the respondents from the buying firm agreed that when there
is a good and open relationship with the suppliers, the buying firm can gain a better
understanding and response to the Oil and Gas market, through the valuable insights
and knowledge that can be obtained and created in these relationships (see quotes from
Senior Negotiator and Contract Manager for SUP1, Category Manager for SUP2, Cat-
egory Manager for SUP3 and Category Manager for SUP5 in Table B.12). It was also
highlighted that this is one aspect where improvements can be made in the collaborative
relationships (Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1 and Senior Negotiator & Contract
Manager for SUP1).
“
I would say, yes, to some extent reflected on my discussions and what
I have. […] Now, the question is to what level? I think neither one
of us leverages as much as we should. […] I don’t think we’ve tapped
into it enough and I think […] the relationship is probably consuming
enough time with SUP1 […] that hasn’t allowed us to do that yet. But
I think there’s room for improvement.
– Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1
”
The interesting finding on the side of the suppliers is that the suppliers consider
that they can offer more to the buying firm in this respect than they can get back.
For instance, a recent example of how they approach the joint knowledge creation was
shared by the Global Key Account Manager for SUP2, who talked about hosting col-
==PAGE:66==
48
Findings
laborative and informative webinars with the buying firm and other clients, in order
to help them form a better understanding of the current market conditions, after the
disruptions caused by COVID-19. The Key Account Manager for SUP3 also explicitly
stated that there is more for the buying firm to gain from them in this regard, rather
than the opposite, because they are working closely with many of the buying firm’s main
competitors also, and therefore he considers that SUP3 have a better understanding of
the industry that they can offer to the buying firm.
“
I would actually say […] that for a lot of the people that we are
speaking to in BF, then we know more about the market. Because we
are dealing with all the other companies as well.
– Key Account Manager for SUP3
”
4.1.6
Overall effect of Supply Chain Collaboration in
the studied relationships
The last question on the interview questionnaire was regarding the respondents’ eval-
uation of the overall effect of the collaboration with the respective companies on the
supply chain performance. Here, the key finding is that the respondents from the sup-
plier companies are generally more prone to evaluate the effect of SCC on supply chain
performance positively than the buying firm ones. Although most of the buying firm
representatives may consider that the collaboration had a positive effect on the overall
performance, they still added a relatively negative comment to that too.
“
I would say it’s overall positive with pockets of negative incidents
here and there as would be expected.
– Senior Negotiator Contract Manager for SUP1
”
The Category Manager for SUP5 recognised that this partner is becoming more col-
laborative and that the buying firm can also improve in some areas in order to achieve
greater efficiencies in performance. The responses obtained from the suppliers’ represen-
tatives were more in line with this idea, with a fairly positive assessment of the impact of
collaboration on the performance of their supply chains, while also noting the presence
of opportunities for further improvements. An overview of the specific quotes can be
found in Table B.13.
==PAGE:67==
4.2 Dyadic Relationships
49
4.2
Dyadic Relationships
In this section, the findings emerging from the direct comparison between the views of
the managers managing the same business relationship are presented. Therefore, the
section is divided in three sub-sections, corresponding to the three relationships that the
author was able to contact both the buying firm and the supplier representatives.
4.2.1
Dyad #1: Buying Firm & Original Equipment
Manufacturer and Spare Parts Provider (OEM &
SPP)
When assessing the relationship between the buying firm and this supplier company
(SUP1), which belongs to the category of Original Equipment Manufacturers and Spare
Parts Providers (OEM & SPP), the general perceptions of the success of the collaboration
were quite different between the two players, with the buying firm being less satisfied
and the supplier company being more satisfied by the overall collaboration.
For instance, when asked about their perceptions on the power asymmetry in the
relationship, the the supplier identified the buyer as more powerful, as they are the final
decision makers, whereas the buying firm identified the supplier as the most powerful
partner due to the high dependency on them, their size and their better market position,
acknowledging that they are also sitting in “a pretty good level of strength right now”
in this relationship. The exact quotes can also be found in Table B.1, as well as in
Appendix C.
As far as the exercise of power in the relationship is concerned, the buying firm
representatives mentioned that the supplier usually exercises power in negotiations, in
order to obtain the most profitable deal for their shareholders, pointing out that this
has been perceived from the buying firm as coercive use of power towards them. On the
other hand, the supplier representative noted that the buying firm exercise their power in
a non-coercive way, and he also shared an example where the buying firm has exercised
power over them by threatening to recertifying some of the supplier’s equipment as their
own in order to reduce the dependency on them and the supplier’s power over the buying
firm. For the specific quotes, the reader is asked to refer to Table B.2 or Appendix C.
Both sides seem to agree that use of power has negative impact on the collaboration,
with the Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1 sharing an example of how the power
exercise from the supplier has impacted the collaboration thereafter, mentioning charac-
teristically that “it leaves a fingerprint on the relationship long term” and the Category
Manager for SUP1 mentioning that they are also looking at other opportunities. The
supplier representative stressed the level of dependence between the two partners by
saying that “they are married to us whether they like it or not, and so, there has to be
collaboration.”, highlighting the need to balance the power in the relationship for the
collaboration to exist (Table B.3).
==PAGE:68==
50
Findings
Although both sides agreed that trust levels vary significantly across the different
hierarchy levels of the two organisations - with the existence of trust being less prominent
in the higher levels, and more prominent at the lower levels of the organisations - however
their views on the foundation of trust in their relationship were quite different, as can
be observed by Table B.4. The Category Manager from the buying firm mentioned the
high dependency on the supplier and the Senior Partnering Manager added to that by
highlighting the absence of utilization of penalties in the relationship with this supplier.
Therefore, from the buying firm side, trust is calculative.
On the other hand, the
Key Account Manager for SUP1 mentioned closeness, openness and credibility as the
foundation where the trust is based with this trading partner, which are all components
of normative trust.
Their views remain divergent about the presence and effect of trust in this collabora-
tive relationship. The supplier company evaluated the impact of trust on the examined
collaboration as “100% positive”, acknowledging that too much trust in a partner can
cause the performance of the relationship to deteriorate due to lack of monitoring, but it
was also mentioned that the buying firm is monitoring them closely. At the same time,
the buying firm assess the same impact rather negatively noting that they tend to keep
feelings of grievance, and disclosed two important aspects that affect the impact of trust
on SCC, namely the fact that the people managing the relationship and building the
trust have been changing too often, and that the buying firm company generally finds
it difficult to appreciate the inestimable benefits of trust and relationship building. The
reader is prompted to Table B.5 or Appendix C if interested in the exact responses.
With regard to the impact of trust on power asymmetry and the exercise of power,
both sides seem to agree that the lack of trust intensifies the power asymmetries in
the relationship, and that signals of trust from both sides have assisted in limiting the
exercise of power (see Table B.6). As far as the impact of power on the existence of trust
in the relationship is concerned, the Senior Partnering Manager from the buying firm
highlighted the long-term negative effects of coercive power on trust using an example
of a time when SUP1 used coercive power in a business deal, and the trust has not been
rebuilt since then. The Key Account Manager from the supplier company also shared
an example of the “power-play” between the two companies, but he explained that “it
has nothing to do with trust in the relationship”. The reader is prompted to Table B.7
for the specific quotes.
Regarding the effect of SCC on the supply chain performance and the aspects of SCC
that are present in this collaborative relationship, the views of the two representatives
varied. On the collaborative communication aspect of SCC, both parties noted that
there is frequent and open communication between the partners the format of which
varies depending on the level of hierarchy, albeit not in the level they would rather
it be. A difference in their views can be observed in the information sharing aspect
of SCC, where the Key Account Manager for SUP1 heavily accentuated the openness
of SUP1 in sharing any kind of relevant information they have with the buying firm
to optimize benefits for both firms - even sensitive market information - whereas the
Category Manager for SUP1 pointed out that the buying firm is more open in infor-
mation sharing than the supplier. It was also mentioned by both sides that there were
==PAGE:69==
4.2 Dyadic Relationships

projects where the two companies shared resources in the past, but not currently. Both
agreed that the decision synchronisation initiatives between the two companies can be
improved, especially from the buying firm’s side and their failure to provide accurate
forecasts to the supplier, whereas, in the aspect of joint knowledge creation, the buying
firm representatives posited that they have improved their knowledge of the market by

4.2.2
Dyad #2: Buying Firm & Third Party Logistics
provider (3PL)
Similarly with the previous dyad, analyzing the relationship between the buying firm and
SUP2 which belongs in the category of Third Party Logistics Providers (3PL), revealed
that the general perceptions on the success of the collaboration were quite different
between the two players, with the buying firm being less satisfied and the supplier
company being more satisfied by the overall collaboration.
More specifically, when asked about their perceptions on the power asymmetry in
the relationship, the buying firm identified the supplier as the most powerful partner:
“they definitely have more leverage than we have due to their size”, whereas the supplier
identified the buyer as more powerful, as they can always “take away their business”, but
also mention that the power is somewhat equal in this relationship. The exact quotes
can also be found in Table B.1, as well as in Appendix C.
As far as the exercise of power in the relationship is concerned, the buying firm
representative considers that it varies from case to case, without sharing any specific
examples. On the other hand, the supplier noted that coercive power can just as well be
“perceived” as such, meaning that one of the two partners may consider that the other
is exercising coercive power in the collaboration. Moreover, the Global Key Account
Manager for SUP2 shared that even though the purchasing firm likes to talk a lot about
partnership and collaboration, when it comes to the way they actually work together
with their suppliers, they are very straightforward and exercise their power, leaving little
leeway to their suppliers. For the specific quotes, the reader is asked to refer to Table B.2
or Appendix C.
Both sides agreed that use of power has negative impact on the collaboration. More-
over, the buying firm representative noted that their company is an attractive client
==PAGE:70==
52
Findings
for SUP2, and therefore if both companies wish the collaboration between them to be
successful, they need to leave any power/leverage outside of the relationship (Table B.3).
The trading partners views on the foundation of trust in their relationship were
also quite different (see Table B.4). The Category Manager from the buying firm cited
reliability and competencies of the supplier (calculative trust) as the foundation where
the trust is based with this trading partner, whereas the Key Account Manager from
the supplier company cited integrity, reliability and openness (cognitive-based trust).
On the existence and impact of trust in this collaborative relationship, their views
continue to be divergent. The supplier company evaluated the impact of trust on the
examined collaboration as “definitely positive”, acknowledging that the negative bits
can be resolved through trust and openness from both parties. On the contrary, the
Category Manager from the buying firm assessed the existence and impact of trust
in this relationship rather negatively, pointing out that “trust has never really been
there in this relationship”, and underlining that “unfortunately” it is the 3PL provider’s
responsibility to build the trust. Moreover, she pointed out that the buying firm tends
to focus on the supplier’s mistakes a lot, whereas they have a “short memory” when the
supplier steps out and provides high quality services. However, she also mentioned that
under the current COVID-19 crisis, this supplier emerged as one of the most reliable
partners for the buying firm. The reader is prompted to Table B.5 or Appendix C if
interested in the exact responses.
With regard to the impact of trust on power asymmetry and the exercise of power,
both sides seem to agree that the existence of trust in the relationship can have a
moderating effect on the use of power and power asymmetry as a whole (see Table B.6).
Their views seem to coincide also regarding the impact of power on the existence of
trust in the relationship, where the Category Manager from the buying firm highlighted
the importance of balancing the power usage, as well as her opinion that when either
party resorts to utilizing their power in the relationship, that is a result of the trust
being broken already in the collaboration.
The Global Key Account Manager from
the supplier company also shared a quite illustrative example, showcasing the negative
impact of power on trust and on the overall collaboration in the longer term.
Regarding the effect of SCC on the supply chain performance and the aspects of SCC
that are present in this collaborative relationship, the views of the two representatives
varied. On the collaborative communication aspect of SCC, both parties noted that there
is frequent and open communication between the partners, whereas in the information
sharing aspect of SCC, there seems to be a misalignment of opinions between them. The
Global Key Account Manager for SUP2 emphasized quite heavily the openness of SUP2
in sharing any kind of relevant information they have with the buying firm to optimize
benefits for both firms, whereas the Category Manager for SUP2 pointed out that they
are not as open as she would have expected from a 3PL provider. She also mentioned that
they are not as open to the sharing of resources as some other 3PL providers. Both agreed
on the lack of decision synchronization in this collaboration, as well as on the notion that
there have been incidents that can be characterized as joint knowledge creation, that
emerged from working together. Lastly, regarding their perception of the overall impact
of this collaboration on their supply chains’ performances, the Global Key Account

Manager for SUP2 evaluated it positively, suggesting some improvements in the trust

4.2.3
Dyad #3: Buying Firm & Service Provider

In this section, the findings of the comparison between the answers of the dyad of
the buying firm with the supplier company SUP3 which belongs in the category of
Service Providers (Manpower) will be presented. The interpretations of the collaboration
performance were generally quite different between the two parties, with the buying
firm being less satisfied and the supplier company being more satisfied with the overall
collaboration.
Firstly, when asked about their perceptions on the power asymmetry in the relation-
ship, the buying firm identified the supplier as the most powerful partner at the moment,
noting that before the COVID-19 crisis both partners stood in the same level of power,
whereas the supplier identified the buyer as more powerful, saying that “we are more
reliant on BF than BF is reliant on us”. The exact quotes can also be found in Table B.1,
as well as in Appendix C.
With respect to the exercise of power in the relationship, the buying firm represen-
tative considers that this supplier stopped treating them as a trusted partner after they
gained more power. On the other hand, the supplier noted that the way the buying firm
use their power is by making them “waste a lot of time” trying to comply with their
ideas, without actually using them afterwards. When asked about the power’s impact
on the collaboration, the Category Manager from the buying firm noted that the overall
outcome of power is negative, whereas the Key Account Manager for SUP3 commented
that the power asymmetry had no impact in the collaboration between the two com-
panies. For the specific quotes, the reader is asked to refer to Table B.2, Table B.3 or
Appendix C.
On the other hand, the trading partners’ views on the foundation of trust in their
relationship were quite similar (see Table B.4). The Category Manager from the buying
firm cited reliability, competencies and reliability of the supplier (calculative trust) as
the foundation where the trust is based with this trading partner, and the Key Account
Manager from the supplier company agreed mentioning that the buying firm always
chooses SUP3 on difficult assignments because of their competences. He also noted that
reward schemes have no effect on the level of trust, as they are purely performance-based.
On the existence and impact of trust in this collaborative relationship, their views
are quite different. The supplier company evaluated the impact of trust on the examined
collaboration as “always been positive”, noting that they struggle with the lack of stability
in the personnel at the Procurement department of the buying firm. On the contrary,
==PAGE:72==
54
Findings
the Category Manager from the buying firm assessed the existence and impact of trust

With regard to the impact of trust on power asymmetry and the exercise of power,

that there is frequent and open communication between the partners, with the supplier

also, and therefore he considers that SUP3 have abetter understanding of the industry

effect of this collaboration on the performance of their supply chains, the buying firm
representative stressed that in this crisis, the supplier did not engage in collaborative
initiatives with them, but rather chose to take advantage of the situation, putting their
relationship with the buying firm in a difficult position, while the Key Account Manager
for SUP3 evaluated it positively, saying that: “we are happy working with BF”, but also
mentioning that he does not think this collaboration has had an impact on their supply

chain. The specific answers of the respondents can be found in Table B.8 - Table B.13,
