4.3
Summary of Key Findings
A summary of the key findings presented in the above sections can be found below:
• In 4 out of the 5 studied relationships, the buying firm considers the supplier
to be most powerful trading partner, mentioning reasons such as the size of the
organisations, the level of dependency and the current market conditions. On the
other hand, the supplier companies seem to agree that the buying firm has more
leverage in the relationship, the reason being that the buying firm is the ultimate
decision maker with regard to whether to collaborate with them or choose one of
their competitors.
• Each of the partners considers that the other makes sure that the most powerful
partner shows their power in the relationship. The buying firm shares some ex-
amples of incidents where the suppliers exercised their power on them. However,
they also highlight that the use of power varies substantially from case to case.
They acknowledge that the overall market conditions are very dynamic and even
cyclical.
• Suppliers consider the buying firm as being the most powerful trading partner
in the relationship and they mention that the buying firm utilises their power
strategically, usually when capital expenditures are concerned or big investment
projects. They use their power by offering ultimatums and making suppliers work
harder to earn their business. Although some suppliers understand the high level
of dependency of the buying firms for their equipment/services, they consider they
never took advantage of that.
• From both the buying firm’s and the suppliers’ perspective, the overall outcome
of use of power is negative in any relationship and that there can be no genuine
collaboration if the partners exercise their respective powers on each other. Buyer
and supplier should leave their power baggage outside the relationship in order
to ensure close collaboration and partnership, according to a representative of the
buying firm. The buying firm try to increase the collaboration efforts in the hopes
of reducing the exertion of power from a powerful supplier.
• An interesting finding emerged from an example in which the use of power was
found to momentarily improve the business performance, but with adverse effects
on the long-term benefits of the collaboration.
• When asked about the type of trust in the studied relationships, the respondents
from the buying firm highlight the high dependence on some of these suppliers.
==PAGE:74==
56
Findings
Regarding the use of rewards or penalties and the effect these have on building
and maintaining the trust between the partners, the general finding is that these
are used very rarely or not at all, and therefore do not have an impact on trust. For
suppliers, closeness, openness and credibility are the most important constructs of
trust that are currently present in the studied relationships and they mention that
although there have been projects where there were rewards, these were purely
performance-based and had no impact on trust.
• Another important finding emerging from the responses of the respondents is that
the level of trust varies significantly in the different hierarchy levels within the
organisations. There is more trust in the lower hierarchy levels (operational and
tactical levels) and much less at the higher levels.
• The buying firm highlights that there is some kind of “false trust’’ in the relation-
ships they have with their partners. They have a difficulty to evaluate the things
that cannot be monetized, and they are generally more reluctant to evaluate posi-
tively the impact of trust on the relationships. Supplier companies seemed to have
a more positive view on the impact of trust in the studied relationships. Despite
the trust, they always get monitored by the buying firm, so the relationship does
not get complacent. The importance of trust and being proactive and transparent
was exemplified by a supplier company.
• The buying firm is a dynamic organisation, where people change positions very
often, especially in Procurement and this instability in the people managing the
relationships has always been a challenge for the buying firm. This is an interesting
finding, especially considering the fact that it is people who build and maintain
the relationships between organisations.
• When asked about the effect of trust on power asymmetry, the answers retrieved
from the buying firm respondents seem to vary depending on the individual rela-
tionship. Some contented that signals of trust have aided in limiting the exercise
of power in the relationships, while others mentioned that it had no significant
effect in the power asymmetry.
• Lack of trust adds to the power dynamics in the relationship. Suppliers have been
using their power more strategically due to the trust shown by the buying firm.
This has led to less exercise of power from both sides, thus limiting the impact of
power asymmetry. Being trustful and responsive can minimize the negative effects
of power in a business relationship.
• The consensus among buying firm and suppliers seems to be that use of coercive
power “breaks” the trust in the relationship. If the trust foundation is solid enough,
moderate use of power in the relationships cannot have a long-term negative out-
come, according to the respondents.
• Communication at the operational level is the most frequent (multiple times per
week), utilizing emails or phone calls and exchanging transactional information and
==PAGE:75==
4.3 Summary of Key Findings
57
updates. The tactical levels (Category/Contract Managers and Key Account Man-
agers) are said to have regular structured calls (for example biweekly or monthly)
to discuss current challenges. At the strategic levels, the communication is even
more structured, with Quarterly Business Reviews (QBR) or biannual meetings.
The aspect of collaborative communication is deemed as a potential area of im-
provement for the buying firm.
• The buying firm is generally open in sharing any kind of information necessary
to their suppliers, apart from commercially sensitive information. The types of
information shared by the buying firm with their suppliers are performance related,
technical and maintenance related, spend numbers and project pipeline. Suppliers
share even market-sensitive information with the buying firm.
• Sharing of resources is treated quite differently depending on the specific studied
relationship. With the biggest and more powerful OEM & SPP suppliers, it was
mentioned that the trading partners have had some projects in the past which
involved sharing of resources.
On the other hand, with the 3PL provider and
the Manpower Service Provider, the joint resources aspect involves collaborative
teams.
• Both the buying firm and suppliers commonly cited that the aspect of decision syn-
chronisation is not at the desired levels. The buying firm commented that they try
to share their project plans in advance with their partners to help them plan their
respective operations. Suppliers mentioned utilizing B2B (Business-to-Business)
solutions to simplify some common processes and had an almost unanimous re-
quest for proper forecasts from the buying firm.
• Suppliers consider that they have a better understanding of the industry, so they
can offer more to the buying firm in this respect than they can get back. They
do that by hosting webinars to help them form a better understanding of the
current market conditions and by sharing information about competitors, as they
are working closely with many of the buying firm’s main competitors. The buying
Firm agrees that when there is a good and open relationship with the suppliers,
they can gain a better response to the Oil and Gas market.
• Supplier companies are more prone to evaluate the effect of SCC on supply chain
performance positively than the buying firm. Buying firm representatives may
consider that the collaboration had a positive effect on the overall performance,
but they still added a relatively negative comment to that too. The responses
obtained from suppliers’ representatives included a more positive assessment of
the impact of collaboration on the performance of their supply chains, but they
also noted the presence of opportunities for further improvements.
==PAGE:76==
58
==PAGE:77==
CHAPTER 5
Discussion
The analysis of the interview data produced some interesting findings, which were pre-
sented in chapter 4. In this chapter, important remarks are discussed regarding the the
implications and interpretations of the findings of this research project, drawing up to
the literature and the research questions.
5.1
Different perspectives in the relationships
Comparing the buying firm’s and suppliers’ perceptions of relationship dimensions, it
was found that there are significant differences in the perception of these dimensions
in the relationship, especially for power asymmetry and most powerful trading partner,
exercise of power in the relationship, impact and existence of trust in the relationship
and overall assessment of supply chain collaboration. This overarching result confirms
previous research and specifically the findings of Ambrose et al. (2010) and Whipple
et al. (2010).
More specifically, regarding power asymmetry and the perception of the most pow-
erful trading partner, the views of the buying firm were diametrically opposite to those
of their suppliers, with each of the partners perceiving the other as more powerful. The
buyer measured the suppliers’ power based on company size, profitability and depen-
dence, whereas the suppliers measured the buying firm’s power based on the number
of their competitors and the buyer’s market position.
This finding is in agreement
with the findings of Ambrose et al. (2010), who argued that buyers and suppliers in
a dyadic relationship have significantly different perceptions of trust, commitment, re-
source dependence, power and relationship success, adding an important aspect: that
of the power-holder in the relationship. The findings also suggest that the buying firm
might consistently underestimate the suppliers’ dependence on themselves, by perceiv-
ing higher levels of buyer dependence (Caniëls et al. 2010). Managers from both sides
of the partnership can benefit from this insight in each other’s perspective, as they can
have more leverage in business transactions and negotiations, that will probably enable
them to obtain better results for their firms.
In a similar fashion, since each of the partners perceives the other as more powerful,
their views on perception and exercise of power (coercive and non-coercive) were also
divergent. Each partner cited the other as utilizing their respective power, either in
negotiations or in big investment projects. This means that supply chain partners can
generally be influenced by the effects of coercive and non-coercive power sources exercised
==PAGE:78==
60
Discussion
by the other partner, as well as by perceptions of the other partner’s power position
even if power sources are not being used. Thus, supporting the findings of Gaski (1984)
and Gaski & Nevin (1985). Generally, if one side perceives the other as coercive, it
would possibly respond by refusing to participate in collaborative initiatives.
These
behaviors indicate little tolerance to coercion. Practical implications of this finding are
that managers from both sides can have a better understanding of how their actions are
perceived by their partners, and therefore adjust their use of power accordingly, in order
to achieve the desired outcomes.
Another area where the views of the buyer and the suppliers are quite different is
that of the type and existence of trust in the relationship. The buying firm’s trust is
usually based on dependency, reliability and competencies of their suppliers, thus ex-
hibiting a calculative and cognitive-based type of trust. Therefore, the buying firm’s
trust is based upon the high dependency and objective credibility of their trading part-
ners, meaning that since their partners are reliable and in possession of high technical
expertise and expertness, they trust that they will therefore act and perform according
to the established agreements between them (Caniëls et al. 2010, Mayer et al. 1995).
Conversely, the suppliers generally mention the openness, closeness and credibility of
the buying firm as the foundation of their trust in the relationship, which is identified
as normative trust. This means that the suppliers’ trust is characterized by a shared
identity between partners and is associated with high levels of commitment and loyalty
in the relationship (Gounaris 2005, Jain et al. 2014). Those two different trust forms
reflect different behavioral conditions. The former represents a desire to sustain the
partnership based on pragmatic criteria, whereas the latter represents a willingness to
preserve the relationship based on optimistic beliefs. This is a beneficial setting for the
managers of the buying firm, as they can get the most out of the collaboration (both
through their objectivity and through their supplier’s commitment). The same cannot
be said for the suppliers, as this type of buyer trust can attenuate the positive effect
of their actions. Therefore, suppliers should try to develop the buyer’s normative trust
in order to gain a more advantageous place in the partnership, by providing incentives,
knowledge sharing, and providing the buying firms with expert advice. This way, the
buyer will tend to overlook the negative actions of their partners seeking to maintain a
positive working atmosphere in the collaboration.
Moreover, another interesting difference in the views of the trading partners is re-
garding the impact of trust on the collaboration. As shown in section 4.1, the buying
firm representatives generally seemed more reluctant to assess positively the impact of
trust on the business relationships studied, evaluating it rather negatively, while the
representatives of the supplier firms seemed to have a more positive evaluation of the
impact of trust on the relationships studied. This finding contradicts those of Corsten &
Kumar (2005), who found that it was the suppliers who felt greater sense of inequity and
dissatisfaction in the relationship, and not the buyers. This finding can be associated
with the above insight regarding the type of trust of each partner, and therefore the
practical implications can be the same, i.e. the suppliers’ attempt to ensure that the
buyer’s trust is also normative.
Attempting to answer the third research question of this study, the analysis of the
==PAGE:79==
5.2 The role of Power in collaborative relationships
61
data revealed that the views on the impact of trust on power asymmetry varied quite
significantly, both between the buying firm and their suppliers, but also within the buy-
ing firm. Lending support to the previous study of Caniëls et al. (2010) who contended
that trust could act as a moderating mechanism to counteract the potential negative
effects of power and dependence asymmetry on the relationship, representatives of the
buying firm and the supplier companies attested that lack of trust intensifies the power
asymmetry and the power dynamics, and that they have experienced less use of power
from their powerful partner because of their signs of trust and commitment. However,
there were also testimonies of no significant impact of the existence of trust in the use
of power. Therefore, further research is needed to investigate different scenarios where
trust can act as a moderator or power exercise in business relationships.
Lastly, a significant contradiction of the views between the buying firm and their
suppliers was present at the topic of the effect of SCC on supply chain performance.
Once again, the suppliers were generally more likely to assess this impact as positive than
the buying firm. This finding is very much in line with those of Ambrose et al. (2010),
Corsaro & Snehota (2011) and Kumar et al. (1998), and is therefore strengthening the
argument that suppliers typically rate the relationship more highly than buyers. This
specific difference in perception can be based on the different expectations of the partners,
and therefore the different criteria they have for success. Thus, managers need to align
on their perceptions of success and increased performance, by setting SMART goals and
KPIs commonly shared with all the supply chain partners, enabling for goal congruence
and incentive alignment to be established in the relationship (Cao & Zhang 2011).
5.2
The role of Power in collaborative
relationships
Several valuable insights were drawn from the findings regarding the instrumental role of
power in collaborative supply chain relationships, answering the first research question
of this thesis project. These findings have both theoretical and practical implications,
which are discussed in detail below.
One of the important findings in this area was that power asymmetry is not static
in the relationship, but instead, since it is based on the dependence of the partners on
each other, it is very much influenced by macroeconomic factors and market conditions.
Therefore, the “power-holders” change, depending on the current phase of the market,
confirming the findings of Ghadge et al. (2017) and Nair et al. (2011). Ergo, effective
relationship management requires managers in both the buyer and the supplier compa-
nies to be wary of their position of power and influence, and to use the appropriate
level of control depending on the power structure of the relationship. By adjusting their
type of control and exercise of power, firms will substantially enhance the collaborative
actions of their supply chain partners and improve their operational efficiency (Nyaga
et al. 2013).
==PAGE:80==
62
Discussion
Another quite significant finding of this project regarding the impact of coercive
power usage (or the perception of it) on supply chain collaboration is that, although it
can improve the performance of the supply chain momentarily, the long term impacts on
collaboration are strongly negative. This means that partners should be very strategic
and vigilant on the occasions where they need to utilize coercive power against their
partners, as the benefits they may experience will most likely be short-lived and they
will end up diminishing the value generating potential of the relationship thereafter. This
was also observed by Nyaga et al. (2013) and Johnsen & Lacoste (2016). In such cases,
non-coercive types of power could be more beneficial (e.g. reward power), as studies
indicate that they can have positive effect on trust and commitment in the collaboration
(Benton & Maloni 2005), which also agrees with another finding of this project, namely
that circumspect use of non-coercive power from partners cannot have long term negative
results on the collaboration.
In the same wavelength, one of the aspects where there was a unanimous agreement
between the buying firm and the suppliers was that the overall outcome of coercive
power is always negative for the trust, answering the fourth research question of this
study. This finding is quite important, supporting the arguments of Cox (2001), Gaski
(1984), Gaski & Nevin (1985) and Jain et al. (2014), and has some significant practical
implications for both the buying firms and the suppliers. More precisely, both buyers
and suppliers should mitigate threatening and imposing harsh penalties and sanctions
on their partners because these can have quite serious negative ramifications on trust
and therefore the collaboration overall.
Although not supported fully by all respondents, it is also suggested by the findings of
this study that engaging in truly collaborative relationships with key suppliers limits the
use of coercive power from both parties. This means that increased levels of trust, loyalty,
openness and transparency foster deeper collaboration between the firms, enabling more
flexibility and new ways of working with each other to achieve mutual goals. Therefore,
trust and collaboration act as discouraging factors for using coercive power by the most
powerful partners (Cowan et al. 2015). This finding is also in line with another important
finding of this project, namely that when partners resort to using their power on each
other, that is a result of losing the trust in each other.
5.3
The role of Trust in collaborative
relationships
Many useful insights have been derived from the results concerning the integral role of
trust in collaborative supply chain relationships, addressing this project’s second research
question. The theoretical as well as managerial implications of these results are discussed
in depth below.
Trust was found to be a critical mechanism for facilitating coordination and collabo-
ration between trading partners, lending support to Huo et al. (2019) and Rogers & Fells
==PAGE:81==
5.3 The role of Trust in collaborative relationships
63
(2017). This has important managerial implications, as trusted partners can therefore
make more efficient use of valuable information, as each party can share openly impor-
tant information allowing their partnership interactions to run seamlessly. Partnering
firms therefore engage more efficiently and cope more effectively with day-to-day chal-
lenges, which then in turn leads to increasing the value of the relationship transactions
and provides them with “coopetitive advantage” (Nair et al. 2011).
What is more, the findings of this study contradict those of Mora-Monge et al.
(2019) and Yeung et al. (2009) regarding the negative impacts of trust on SCC. More
specifically, the existence of trust in the studied business relationships did not reduce
the monitoring of the partners and did not create any kind of discriminatory treatment
of potentially better trading partners, and therefore, did not make the relationship
complacent. This adds even more value to the previous argument that the existence of
trust is crucial and fundamental in collaborative relationships, by accentuating the lack
of negative implications associated with it. Nonetheless, managers ought to be cautious
about this possibility and ensure a reasonable degree of mutual monitoring to prevent
these detrimental outcomes of too much trust.
As previously mentioned, the analysis of the interview data revealed that the degree
of trust varied significantly across the various levels of hierarchy within the organisations.
This means that there is generally more trust at the lower levels of the organisations
and less trust at the higher hierarchy levels (senior leadership and C-suite management),
which can be explained by the inherently different degree of openness, frequency of
communication and amount of collaboration between those levels. To the best of the
author’s knowledge, no other incidents of this finding have been documented in the
trust literature, making this an important contribution, also to the management practice.
Organisations should identify how big these discrepancies in trust levels are and act upon
them, to minimize the adverse effects these low trust levels of the higher management
can have if they disseminate on the lower ones.
Moreover, the findings indicate that trust is seen as being developed and maintained
between people, supporting the findings of the extant literature (Caniëls et al. 2010,
Kwon & Suh 2004, Rogers & Fells 2017). This poses a critical challenge for the buying
firm, which was described as a “very dynamic” organisation, where people change po-
sitions very frequently, thereby not allowing for the managers to develop and maintain
the desired levels of trust in the relationships they manage. Hence, it is imperative that
supplier relationship management should be well aligned with the overarching strategic
goals of the supply chain department and the organisation as a whole, if the companies
wish to benefit from engaging in collaborative partnerships.
A quite interesting finding about the buying firm’s trust emerged from the analysis
of the interview data, that is that the buying firm’s highly calculative trust does not
allow for deep trust (normative trust) to be developed between them and their partners.
As a result, they do not allow their partners to make any mistakes and they tend to stick
with negative past experiences more than they appreciate the positive ones. This inhibits
their efforts for supply chain collaboration, since trust is an integral part of collaborative
partnerships. Rogers & Fells (2017) argued that the trust between buyer and supplier
will be mainly self-interested and calculative in the first phases of forming an exchange
==PAGE:82==
64
Discussion
relationship, but in the studied relationships, the trust does not seen to have developed
from this phase (from the buying firm’s perspective). Therefore, procurement managers
should be wary of this irregular characteristic of their partnerships and act upon it,
by engaging in relationship building activities with the managers from the partnering
organisations.
According to the literature, using rewards and penalties is an effective way to in-
centivise the partners to work towards the best interests of the exchange relationship.
Nyaga et al. (2013) and Zhao et al. (2008) found that establishing and using rewards and
incentives in the partnership can be a measurable indication from the partners that they
are in a long-term partnership and therefore that they respect the relationship, making
the partners feel that they have a say in partnerships. However, the findings of this
study indicated that although such systems are in place, they are hardly ever used in
practice, because - according to the respondents - falling back to such incentives is a last
resort and a sure way to turn the collaborative relationship into a purely transactional
one. A reason for this might be that the respondents generally had in mind penalties
rather than rewards when explaining the damaging effects on collaborative relationships.
Hence, a practical recommendation emerging from this finding is that managers should
focus more on establishing and using performance-based rewards and for their partners
(such as increasing the spend with them or offering discounts), in order to increase the
levels of trust and “buy-in” in the partnerships rather than resorting to settling penal-
ties (such as canceling orders or settling late payment fees). Such incentives can boost
reciprocity and collaboration, as the recipients are more likely to meet the requirements
of their partner in order to continue to receive rewards. This can be utilized at the first
stages of the partnership (Rogers & Fells 2017), to establish calculative trust, which
consequently can be turned into normative trust by engaging in relationship building
activities as discussed above.
Lastly, it is worth mentioning that the analysis of the interview data indicated that
in order for businesses to achieve effective collaboration, openness and transparency,
credibility and trust are required to be built and maintained in the relationship. This
underscores the criticality of the existence of trust in these business relationships, and its
role as antecedent for successful supply chain collaboration (Ambrose et al. 2010, Doney
& Cannon 1997). Thus, practitioners should focus their efforts in building trust with
their supply chain partners in order to achieve high levels of openness and transparency
and therefore successful supply chain collaboration.
5.4
Assessment of the supply chain
collaboration
Regarding the degree of supply chain cooperation and the key SCC structures present in
the relationships examined, a lot of valuable insights were extracted from the findings,
==PAGE:83==
5.4 Assessment of the supply chain collaboration
65
answering the fifth and sixth research questions of this project. The theoretical and
managerial implications of these findings are discussed in further detail below.
Regarding the collaborative communication aspect of SCC, the following conclusions
can be drawn from the analysis of the data. Firstly, the communication between the
buying firm and the suppliers is quite frequent and regular at operational and tactical
levels, while it is much less frequent and more structured at the strategic levels. This
type of collaborative communication indicates a close relationship between the suppliers
and the buying firm at the lower hierarchy levels, and a more formal and impersonal
at the higher ones, confirming the literature (Cao & Zhang 2011, Maestrini et al. 2018).
Moreover, frequent communication between partners was found to offer opportunities to
build trust (as part of openness), which also contributes to reciprocal commitment in
collaborative supply chains. However, it was also acknowledged by both the buying firm
and the suppliers that communication between them can still be improved and that the
frequent change of people managing the relationships inside the organizations affects
the continuity of communication.
Therefore, the effectiveness of the communication
channels used should be taken unto consideration by the managers of the buying firm,
both personally and across the organisation. Since effective communication is especially
significant to the suppliers, relatively little effort on the part of the buying firm to
improve communication could reap substantial advantages in the performance of their
business relationships.
Looking at the aspect of information sharing, the processing of the interview data
suggested that both the buying firm and suppliers are open in sharing of information
with each other, and more specifically project pipeline, spend numbers and inventory
levels that align well with the findings of Hudnurkar et al. (2014) and Baihaqi & Sohal
(2013). Nonetheless, it was also determined from the interviews that the suppliers are
more willing and open in sharing also more sensitive information in comparison with
the buying firm that shares only the necessary information and protects any sensitive
information. This finding is very much in line with the findings of Lee & Ha (2018),
who argued that buyers consider that they may obtain sufficient information from their
suppliers, but are often unwilling to provide their suppliers with information themselves.
In general, all respondents agree that information sharing is a significant building block
for SCC, contributing to trust and cooperation, improving proactive problem solving,
facilitating innovation and long-term sustainability, which in turn contribute to increased
supply chain efficiency and performance as a whole (Baihaqi & Sohal 2013, Doney &
Cannon 1997, Hudnurkar et al. 2014).
As far as the resources sharing aspect of SCC is concerned, the findings of this
study indicated that there have been projects where the partners shared resources too,
both in terms of capital, physical and human resources (Harland et al. 2004), however
not in a very advanced level. Drawing up to the arguments of Samaddar & Kadiyala
(2006) regarding factors that promote successful SCC, efficient allocation and sharing
of resources is deemed critical for any successful collaboration, and organizations should
be encouraged to work together towards knowledge creation.
The decision synchronization aspect of SCC in the studied relationships was found
to be inadequate by both the suppliers and the buying firm. Some of the suppliers
==PAGE:84==
66
Discussion
have introduced B2B solutions, simplifying some of the procurement processes for the
buying firm, but the buying firm does not help the demand and production planning
and scheduling of their suppliers due to their inability to provide them with forecasts
about their anticipated purchases and projects (Chopra et al. 2013). This means that
measures to boost these procedures should be taken by the supply chain partners, such
as open discussions and sharing of information regarding upcoming projects and repairs,
and retrieval of historical data from both the buying firm and the supplier companies,
that would therefore enable the buying firm to create more accurate forecasting tools to
share with their partners.
With regard to joint knowledge creation, i.e.
the degree to which supply chain
partners work together to gain a deeper understanding of and response to demand and
the competitive environment (Samaddar & Kadiyala 2006), the key finding was that
suppliers had more to offer to the buying firm than the other way around, supported
by the opinions of both the buyer and the supplier companies. This means that there
are knowledge exploitation mechanisms (i.e. knowledge assimilation and application) in
the studied relationships (Harland et al. 2004), where the buying firm is the partner
that benefits the most from. Improving the joint knowledge creation aspect of SCC
from the buying firm side will create additional incentives for the suppliers to invest in
collaborative initiatives and boost the overall performance of SCC.
Finally, the overarching finding regarding the impact of SCC on the supply chain
performance of the partners was that there are opportunities for improvements from
both sides of the collaboration and for all aspects of SCC. The diverging evaluations were
discussed in detail in section 5.1, and the specific recommendations for improvements
and the managerial implications of the findings were discussed above. Ultimately, these
aspects can provide managers with valuable guidelines for achieving better relationship
development, management and performance.
In order to ensure that the supply chain as a whole performs well, companies should
try to create a win-win situation. Long-term partnerships ought to be driven by shared
interests, congruence of objectives and profit sharing (Simatupang & Sridharan 2002).
According to Carr & Pearson (1999), managers need to align objectives with supply chain
partners in order to create a collaborative advantage that directly increases the financial
performance of each partner in the chain. This can also become an effective means of
transferring knowledge, innovation and new technical skills across firms. Collaboration
is great, but is usually failing because it is not well implemented. Companies really
have to invest time and resources in order to make it work for their benefit. However,
collaboration may also result in increased cost of teamwork and inflexibility, meaning
that managers will need to find the right balance and the optimum degree of collaborative
efforts for their organisations.
==PAGE:85==
CHAPTER 6
Limitations & Suggestions
for Future Research
Several limitations should be noted, that need to be considered when interpreting the
results of this study.
First of all, this study has limited universality, particularly because of the central
importance of the buying firm in research study. The research design included only
long-term, high-value partnerships of a central buying firm in the Oil and Gas industry.
Before attempting to generalize the results of this study, one should evaluate whether
they are unique to certain types of relationships, to the buying firm, to this industry, or
to a combination of all three.
Second, as mentioned in chapter 3, several restrictions and unprecedented events
following the COVID-19 pandemic and the historically low oil prices that shook the
Oil and Gas industry, did not allow for the desired amount of data to be collected in
the tight available time-frame. More specifically, while all interviews were conducted
with the managers of the buying firm, only 3 of 5 of the suppliers’ representatives were
reached and interviewed. This poses a significant limitation in the research, one which
is well acknowledged and taken account in the validity of the findings that are presented
in later chapters.
Data limitations were also imposed due to the case study of only one buying firm with
its strategic suppliers. Although this buying firm can be considered as a representative of
the sector of drilling contractors, it cannot be considered as representative of the whole
Oil and Gas industry companies. It is therefore suggested that future research should
attempt answering these research questions using a bigger sample of buyer-supplier dyads
in the O&G industry. This sample could be determined either from the buyer side, i.e.
testing the relationships of a number of buying firms with their respective strategic
suppliers, or from the supplier side, i.e. testing the relationships of a number of supplier
companies with some of their major customers.
A limitation regarding the data collection method that is also related to the COVID-
19 pandemic and the social distancing measures taking place globally, is that all these
interviews were conducted using an online conferencing platform and not ‘face-to-face’.
This means that the author had limited control over the readiness of the subjects and
the suitability of the environment.
For future studies it is suggested that researchers should use multiple methods to
obtain data, such as questionnaires combined with in-depth interviews, and maybe even
basing the questions on specific scenarios. Additionally, inaccuracy and errors can be
==PAGE:86==
68
Limitations and Suggestions for Future Research
produced when only one respondent is being used and expected to represent broader
variables in the supply chain. In this study, there were multiple respondents from the
buying firm, but only one from each supplier company. Thus, in the future, researchers
should seek to use multiple respondents from each organization participating in the
collaborative relationship, in order to maximize the reliability of the research findings.
Moreover, an “industry-based” analysis of supply chain collaboration would be very
beneficial. Examining how trust and power impact the collaboration and performance
in the relationship, how these mechanics are used/evaluated across buyer-supplier dyads
from different industries, and and what is the most common level of collaboration in the
supply chain in each industry would help identify any industry-specific bias towards or
against collaboration between supply chains.
==PAGE:87==
CHAPTER 7
Conclusion
The objective of this thesis was the investigation of the role of power and trust in
the performance of collaborative dyadic buyer-supplier relationships in the oil and gas
sector. The research was mainly driven by the limited existing knowledge concerning
the interplay of both power and trust in dyadic buyer-supplier relationships, even more
so in the chosen industry sector.
Reviewing the extant literature on supply chain collaboration and relationship man-
agement, it was deduced that trust, power, supply chain collaboration and their effect
on cooperation and success in the supply chain have generally been independently ex-
amined. It was therefore concluded that additional work was required to untangle the
relative influences of trust, power and SCC on supply chain performance. Moreover, it
was found that most of the studies focus on either the buyer side or the supplier side.
Hence, it was considered insightful to examine buyer-supplier dyads, where one player
remains the same (case study of the relationships of a buying firm or a supplier firm).
The research methodology that was followed was to conduct an extensive literature
review of relevant academic sources, to develop a conceptual framework, to identify a case
company and to conduct semi-structured interviews. The data was analysed by applying
qualitative thematic analysis tools to obtain the final findings. These findings were then
discussed in connection with the research questions of this project, the literature review
and the management practices.
Answering the research questions of this study, some of the key findings are summa-
rized below:
1. How does power affect supply chain collaboration?
Firstly, it was found that the perceptions of the buyer and their suppliers were dia-
metrically opposite regarding the most powerful trading partners in the relationship
and how they exercise their power. In addition, from both the buying firm’s and the
suppliers’ perspective, the overall outcome of use of power was considered negative in
any relationship and both stressed that there can be no genuine collaboration if the
partners exercise their respective power on each other. Lastly, an interesting finding
regarding this research question emerged from an example in which the use of power
was found to momentarily improve the business performance, but with adverse effects
on the long-term benefits of the collaboration.
==PAGE:88==
70
Conclusion
2. How does trust affect supply chain collaboration?
Examining the trust in the relationship, it was found that the types of trust between the
buying firm and their suppliers were different. More specifically, the buying firm’s trust
was based on dependency, reliability, and competencies of their suppliers (calculative
trust), while the suppliers’ trust was characterized by a shared identity between partners,
associated with high levels of commitment and loyalty (normative trust). Furthermore,
the degree of trust varied significantly across the various levels of hierarchy within the
organisations.
This can be explained by the inherently different degree of openness,
frequency of communication and amount of collaboration between those levels. Lastly,
another important finding in this area was that there is a high degree of inconsistency
in the people managing the supplier relationships in the Procurement department of
the buying firm, and this has always been a challenge for them, hindering the potential
benefits of trust, especially considering the fact that it is people who build and maintain
the relationships between organisations.
3. How does trust affect the use/perception of power in a business
relationship?
Regarding the effect of trust on power asymmetry, the answers retrieved from the buy-
ing firm respondents varied significantly depending on the individual relationship. Some
contented that signals of trust have aided in limiting the exercise of power in the relation-
ships, while others mentioned that it had no significant effect in the power asymmetry.
However, the general finding underlying the responses was that lack of trust adds to the
power dynamics in the relationship, and that being trustful and responsive can minimize
the negative effects of power in a business relationship. Moreover, there have been cases
where trust has led to less exercise of power from both sides, thus limiting the impact
of power asymmetry. Further research is considered to be needed though, to investigate
different scenarios where trust can act as a moderator or power exercise in business
relationships.
4. How does power affect the presence/type of trust developed in
a business relationship?
Concerning the effect of power on trust, there was a consensus among the buying firm
and suppliers that use of coercive power has a strongly negative impact,“breaking” the
trust in the relationship. Another interesting finding in this area was that if the trust
foundation is solid enough, moderate use of non-coercive power in the relationships
cannot have a long-term negative outcome, according to the respondents.
==PAGE:89==
Conclusion
71
5. Which components of SCC are present in collaborative supply
chains and how do they affect the overall collaboration?
First, the communication between the buying firm and suppliers was found to be quite
frequent and regular at operational and tactical levels, and less regular at the strategic
levels.
Second, regarding information sharing, the suppliers were found to be more
willing to share more sensitive information with the buying firm, while the buying firm
was often unwilling to provide their suppliers with information themselves. This has an
impact also in decision synchronisation, where it was found that the buying firm does not
help the demand and production planning and scheduling of their suppliers by aligning
with them or providing them with forecasts. As far as the resources sharing aspect of
SCC is concerned, the findings of this study indicated that there have been projects
where the partners shared resources, however, not in a very advanced level. Regarding
the joint knowledge creation aspect of SCC, the suppliers were found to have more to
offer to the buying firm than the other way around.
6. What is the effect of SCC on the overall supply chain
performance?
Regarding the overall effect of SCC, the suppliers were more likely to provide a positive
assessment than the buying firm, supporting the argument that suppliers rate the rela-
tionship more highly than buyers. This specific difference in perception can be based on
the different expectations of the partners, and therefore the different criteria they have
for success.
These findings have some important theoretical implications, proving but also con-
tradicting some of the literature studies reviewed for this project, as well as practical
implications that can help managers optimize their supplier relationship management
efforts. Specifically:
• Managers from both sides of the partnership can benefit from the insight in each
other’s perspectives, and have a better understanding of how their actions are
perceived by their partners, and therefore adjust their use of power accordingly, in
order to achieve the desired outcomes.
• Effective relationship management requires managers in both the buyer and the
supplier companies to be wary of their position of power and influence. Partners
should be very strategic and vigilant on the occasions where they need to utilize
coercive power against their partners. In such cases, non-coercive power could be
more beneficial, as studies indicate that it can have positive effect on trust and
commitment in the collaboration.
• Suppliers should try to develop the buyer’s normative trust in order to gain a more
advantageous place in the partnership.
==PAGE:90==
72
Conclusion
• Trust, loyalty, openness, and transparency foster deeper collaboration between the
firms, enabling more flexibility and new ways of working with each other to achieve
mutual goals. Trust and collaboration act as discouraging factors for using coercive
power by the most powerful partners.
• Since the degree of trust varied significantly across the various levels of hierarchy
within the organisations, firms should identify how big these discrepancies in trust
levels are and act upon them.
• Measures to boost the information sharing and decision synchronisation procedures
should be taken by the supply chain partners.
• Improving the joint knowledge creation aspect of SCC from the buying firm’s
side will create additional incentives for the suppliers to invest in collaborative
initiatives. This will boost the overall performance of the SCC and improve the
overall business performance.
Finally, this study was subject to various limitations, which were outlined in chap-
ter 6, together with some suggestions for future research. Overall, it is deemed that this
study successfully managed to answer the research questions and offered some valuable
insights in the trust and power dynamics in dyadic buyer-supplier relationships.

APPENDIX A
Project Plan
This work is the culmination of the author’s efforts to obtain an MSc degree in Transport
and Logistics at DTU. Reflecting on the planning and execution of this project, one thing
that can be said for sure, is that certain things are so unpredictable, that nobody can
be prepared for them.
The official initiation date of this thesis project was the 27th of January 2020, with
5.5 months ahead until the final deadline for submitting the final report. As early as
the 29th of the same month, the author handed in a first draft of the project plan, which
was quite abstract in its form, but was used as a guide to keep track of the progress of
the project. In this first project plan, the basic activities of the thesis were laid out, and
six “milestone dates” were marked, where major meetings would take place between the
author and the supervisors of this project, apart from the established weekly meetings.
The first and second milestone meetings were executed at the agreed dates and the
overall project seemed well “on-track”, until the COVID-19 pandemic and the general
lockdown measures that took place globally around mid-March. Such an unprecedented
event had of course a big impact on this project for several reasons, such as:
• Meetings with the supervising team had to be held virtually and much less often,
resulting in reducing the number of “milestone meetings” from 6 to 4.
• The increased workload and the fact that people were working from home greatly
complicated the data collection process, as the majority of the interviews had
to be rescheduled up to 3 times, and some were even rendered impossible to be
conducted on time.
• Significant fluctuations in the productivity levels of the author were observed,
directly linked to the effects of the lockdown measures and the pandemic.
Nonetheless, agility and high drive from both the author and the supervision team
enabled the author to complete the project on time. The revised and much more detailed
project plan can be found below, with a layout of the different activities and tasks that
had to be completed in order to accomplish the objectives of this thesis project. The
biggest discrepancies between the initial project plan and the revised one can be found in
the “Data collection” and “Analysis” activities, where the time-span of these activities
is larger and overlapping with each other.
==PAGE:102==
84
Appendix A Project Plan
All in all, it was a challenging but also exciting project.
The main reflection after
this extensive work is completed, is that sometimes things happen that are out of our
immediate control and we must adapt to these changes and do our best, even if we know
that it will not be as perfect as we had initially hoped for. Despite the limitations of
this work which are well acknowledged, I am very satisfied with the final result and the
overall learning experience of managing a 5-month project.

Interview #1:  
One-on-one Interview with: 
• J. P. M.: Category Manager for SUP1 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Hi J. P. M. I will just share my screen a little bit so we can go through the questions one by one and you can 
provide your insights. If you can answer some of them. I should also let you know that I'm recording this session 
to get my notes transcribed later. So, then the first question it's regarding the power of the two players so BF 
and SUP1. So, for example, who is the most powerful player in this relationship?  
 
J. P. M.   
Yes, so SUP1 definitely. They are definitely in a more powerful position than us. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
And do you think that they, they actually exercise this power against BF or..? 
 
J. P. M.   
Yes, I think so. I mean we're just one other client for them and we're not a big one. So, we're not even a key 
client for them. So, they, I don't know, in any business or any deal that we get to have with them, they push a 
lot. They try to get as much profit as they can get out of the business rather than developing a partnership 
with us. They rather try to obtain as much profitability as possible from their side. So just an example, right now 
we're dealing with the latest project so yeah some analytical system in order to identify any issue with our BOP 
stack and they are just pushing too much, they're selling us this technology but also they're charging us a 
certain percentage of the savings we get with that technology so like, that's kind of too much. It's like you're 
sending a car that saves fuel but I'm going to charge you for the fuel you get to save something like that. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Okay. And would you say that this power exertion from their side has, in a way, affected the relationship with 
them, negatively or positively? 
Commented [AS1]: Power on SCC: 
The most powerful partner (the supplier) uses their 
power over the weakest (buyer) during negotiations and 
in any deal, they just push a lot to achieve the most 
profitable results for their company. 
 
Most powerful partner: 
The supplier is more powerful. 
Commented [AS2]: Power on SCC: 
 
Overall effect: 
The two companies have a big bond, but lately the 
focus is to explore other options for the products and 
services they provide because the buyer is not very 
happy with the collaboration.

J. P. M.   
So far, I think that we have big bond with them for the services like the well control services. But I think right 
now there is more focus in, and we are also seeing some other opportunities and then other third parties might 
supply the service so we're exploring other options right now. But before and until recently, we were going 
exclusively for them.  
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Good. Okay. And then we also want to investigate the effect of trust in supply chain collaboration. And then, 
my question is Would you say that there is a sufficient amount of trust between BF and SUP1, and is this trust 
based on, for example, some rewards or penalties or, mostly in dependency that BF has on SUP1? 
 
J. P. M.   
I think they see the relationship more like that, like you said, there's a dependency from us to them. So, we

cancellations penalties for late payments, but not too many things if they deliver late. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Okay. And then the overall collaboration, is it an open collaboration or do they tend to be more closed up 
and do not share information that much? 
 
J. P. M.   
They tend to be more closed up.  
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Okay. And would you assess this as a negative effect on the collaboration or positive? 
 
J. P. M.   
Well, so far, my experience has been rather negative. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Great. And the next question is, how would you interpret the effect of trust on the power asymmetry in this 
relationship? 
 
J. P. M.   
Can you explain that. 
 
Commented [AS3]: Trust on SCC: 
 
There is trust in the relationship, but it is based on the 
high dependency of the buyer to the supplier. There are 
penalties, but they are never used.  
 
The supplier is not as open as the buyer would like to 
be, negatively affecting the collaboration. 
 
In the respondent’s experience, the trust has too little to 
have a positive effect on the collaboration. 
 
 
Commented [AS4]: Trust on Power: 
 
The buyer’s trust and loyalty towards the supplier has 
alleviated the use of power from the supplier in the 
relationship.

Interview #2: 
Focus group Interview with: 
• J. M.: Senior Negotiator & Contract Manager for SUP1 
• D. F.: Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Hi D.F. and J. M. Thank you for participating in this interview. So, first of all, an Introduction from me. I'm currently 
doing my master's thesis at DTU, where I am studying Transport and Logistics, and then the thesis project title 
is the role of trust and power in supply chain collaboration, and how this affects supply chain performance. 
And we're doing this in collaboration with BF a little bit so we're looking into some key suppliers, strategic and 
operational ones. And we want to drill down a little bit in these relationships and see how trust and power, 
have an effect in the collaboration and their overall performance. So, the first group of questions is regarding 
the effect of power on supply chain collaboration. But before that, I would like an introduction from you 
regarding SUP1, because I'm not very sure that I understand the company overall and how they have 
become one company. So, if you could spend a few minutes explaining this to me that would be great. 
 
D. F.   
Sure, of course. So, a little history of the company that you refer to as "company x" in your questionnaire.  It's 
been through an iteration of a number of I would say acquisitions or ownership changes or structural changes 
within the H. brand. So, our relationship from BF to SUP1, as what it's known today, started with the H. brand. 
That H. brand produces subsea equipment, predominantly blowout preventers which we refer to that as a 
BOP, and our relationship goes all the way back to the "R-rigs" when we first built the "R-rigs", we equip them 
with the SUP1, or the at that time the H. blowout preventers. As years transpired, we started a new-build 
program with our Deepwater fleet, first building the Deepwater semies, and upon that we went to tender with 
our two main suppliers being SUP5 and SUP1, where we chose SUP1 - at that time H. (again I'm sorry, I keep 
referring to their current name), but H. was chosen to be the equipment provider for our Deepwater fleet at 
that time, which was the "D-rigs". So, we not only, so we chose H. to provide the blowout preventers, but A. - 
a different company - to provide the risers. Fast forward a few more years when we started our new build 
program with the, with the "V-rigs". During the period of selecting the "D-rigs" to the time where we built the 
"V-rigs", H. was acquired by G. The H. brand, along with a number of other components was acquired by the 
G. organization as they were expanding in their whole oil and gas division. With that, then eventually, there 
was a merger, where G. merged with SUP1, they - some call it an acquisition, but it was known as a merger - 
where G. had a majority share of the ownership of SUP1. Upon that merger, the H. oilfield equipment was 
placed under the SUP1 brand. And then, eventually GE decided to exit from the oil and gas industry. That's 
when it was then called "SUP1 a G. company", now it's just SUP1. So that's where we have the name today, 
but the majority of the relationship resides with the H. brand, and for the "V-rigs", it also includes the V. brand, 
which was also part of the acquisition of G. So, does that make sense? 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI   
Yes, it's a lot clearer to me right now because I had no idea about all these changes and how it became one 
company and then split again, all these things before so 
 
D. F.   
Commented [AS12]: History of the Hydril-Baker 
Hughes-GE corporation and background.

One-on-one Interview with: 
• D. F.: Senior Partnering Manager for SUP1 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Hi D. F. Thanks for joining me on a second separate session. So, I made this presentation to make it easier for 
me to switch through the questions and make sure that we are both on the same page. Okay, so I'm going 
to jump right into it. And as you can recall the first group of questions about the effect of power supply chain 
collaboration, and how you would describe the power asymmetry in their relationship with SUP1. So, would 
you like to say anything more on that or do you think that it's been covered already? 
 
D. F.   
No, I think the important element here is that the power asymmetry is very market driven. And it's also very 
much that we can't deny the fact that we have with all of our OEM suppliers, whether it's SUP1, SUP5, there is 
a high level of dependency, because they are the original equipment manufacturer. So, once you acquire 
that equipment there becomes a high high level of dependency for at least the first few years of the 
equipment's life. One is because it's new to us because of warranty three is because you're very dependent 
on working out the kinks and making sure that it runs optimally. And most of our customers want to ensure that 
the equipment we provide them has some reliability to it, and therefore they - our customers - also expect 
that we have a close collaboration with our OEM suppliers to ensure that reliability is solid throughout these 
drilling campaigns, and especially at the time when we bought the equipment originally, there were long 
term contracts - we're talking about three, three to five year contracts. Now, the markets change right the 
market dynamics are different most contracts are short, we're doing what we can to reduce cost, and they're 
feeling that throughout the industry, maybe in the OEMs like SUP1. And so, the market dynamics change so 
the asymmetry of power changes a little bit. So, it kind of sits a little bit with us as the consumer. However, it 
doesn't take away from the high-level dependency. So, I think that that's one piece that I think J. M.  alluded 
to it pretty well in the first interview. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Right. Yes, I agree. And I would just like to have a small follow up on that because last time you highlighted 
that it's very dynamic, and it depends on the oil price and on the oil industry overall, who is going to be the 
strongest partner in this relationship. So sometimes it might be SUP1 and sometimes it might be BF. Is that 
correct? 
 
D. F.

because I've done so. And I've been engaged, which means you have to build that trust not just on the lowest 
level, but all the way up to the image. I always say, “proof is in the pudding”, meaning you can talk the talk, 
but can you walk the walk? And so, what I've been able to do and what SUP2 tries to do is make the proof in 
the pudding. We want to do more than just talk. We want to reflect it. And we show it both in our collaboration 
and open discussion and being transparent. And then working together to find the right solution. So, that's 
what it is: it's been building credibility, building trust and having open communication and transparency. But 
once again, going back, this collaboration must be based not on dependency or rewards penalties, but on 
integrity, reliability, and openness, which all have a positive effect on the trust in the collaboration overall.

K. C.

transparency, discontent, we're going to do it. We're clapping and we apologize, but these are the steps 
we're going to make. That way we will continue to have a positive outcome. Whether even though there's a 
negative connotation in that you didn't meet the target deliberately, at least you come forward with them, 
somewhat proactive process. That's another thing that's so key in trusted relationships, is that you don't be 
reactive but proactive. Being reactive, leads in negative trust and credibility with your customer. We struggle 
with that from time to time. But I'm a very big believer in taking the steps up front, making sure you monitor, 
making sure you can communicate, you are transparent, and you will continue to have a very good 
partnership as long as you want and of course as long as you can stay competitive in the market.

Page 62 of 70 
 
Thank you very much. And do you know of any examples of projects where both companies were sharing 
resources, or they invested capital in a shared project? 
 
J. M. 
I think in the project that I was involved is also a good example of what T. H.  was saying with first access to 
technology. So, today if you are a drilling contractor, you have to - as part of your special periodic survey 
(SPS) of the rig every five years - you have to recertify certain equipment. You need to do like a whole check. 
If it's a jack-up rig you need to open the spud cans and do an examination to see that the steel is still good 
enough for another five years to be recertified. So, this was one of the first big projects at least that I'm aware 
of and that I was involved in, where we did it both on the Deepwater and on the Jack-up side, and that was 
to go out to the to these companies and say: “We don't want to take the rig out of service, put it in a yard or 
even take it out of service offshore to do these recertifications. We would like to see if there's a smarter way 
can we do it while the rig is in operation, and while we're being paid by the client”. And today, in many cases, 
you can do it using divers to do the UIDD, which stands for Underwater Inspection in view of Dry Docking 
(UIDD). So, you'd use divers to go and do the inspection, but normally if there's a diver going down then you 
can't be in full operation. So, we tried to challenge not only SUP4 but all their competitors to say, “Could you 
use an underwater Remote Operated Vehicle plus some robotic arms and stuff, to be able to do this?”. And 
on the deep water I think they concluded that it was possible, but not on the Jack-up's because the robot on 
the ROV could not open the door of the spud can which is the leg, the foot of the jacking legs. And because 
it's what you call a "man hold" I think, where you have to turn it like a steering wheel to open the hatch. And 
unfortunately, that was built for humans are for divers to do, but not built for robots so we could not do it. And 
SUP4 was one of the companies, I would say not the most innovative, but they have the largest amount of 
ROVs and robots, of any company out there that does offshore work. And that was their strongest thing. They 
did have some innovations, but there were some smaller companies that were even more innovative. I don't 
know if that helps answer.

Interview #10: 
One-on-one Interview with: 
• G. M.: Category Manager for SUP5 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Thank you for joining me for this interview today. So, let’s get started with the interview questions which are 
the same as the ones that I attached in the meeting invitation. There are five groups of questions, each one 
investigating the different aspects of the collaboration that we are interested in in this project. The first group 
of questions is about the effect of power on supply chain collaboration, and we would like to know, how would 
you describe the power asymmetry in your relationship with SUP5? Who is the strongest trading partner and 
how is this power exercised to the weakest counterpart? And if you could maybe start with a little bit of 
background in the relationship. 
 
G. M.   
Of course. So, they are one of our identified critical suppliers. They are top suppliers to the buying firm (BF) 
and have been probably for the last 20 years the designer and manufacturer of the majority of the equipment 
that we have on board that's relating to drilling. So, all of the drilling packages that we have on the rigs are 
(with the exception of four) from SUP5, so that means we're very much tied with them. When we did build the 
rigs, we had the options to choose one of three different providers and for 80% of the rigs then we chose SUP5 
as the provider. So obviously we buy their equipment that is manufactured and designed by them. So, that 
means they have much more power over us than we have over them, and they can certainly dictate things: 
they can affect the uptime and the performance of our rig. Because when it comes to software activities if 
the software fails, sometimes we can't utilize the equipment which then means we can't drill, so they can 
have a fundamental impact on the revenue that we can make as an organization. So, the power sits in their 
hands there about our actual equipment, and a lot of our competitors’ equipment also. And we are not the 
biggest drilling contractor, I think we are the fifth or sixth largest now in the market. So, you can see our power 
is quite limited in comparison to if we look at the biggest players in the market. T. for example, they are four 
times the size of BF. So, our approach has been to try and not use the size of our organization as a strength 
but maintain communication, collaboration and then development with the supplier. So, we try to work 
together with them to improve the efficiency for us and for them and to reduce cost, both for them and for 
us as a sales relationship. We try and compete with our competitors and spend a lot more money with them, 
purely on the collaboration aspect by involving them in digital initiatives, so we're using third party companies 
to work together with them. So, I think they more or less have a monopoly over ourselves we have some 
company decisions to take and that's whether or not we want to spend, and buy all of our spare parts from 
SUP5. They themselves rely on a lot of other manufacturers to produce parts, and then SUP5 uses these parts 
to then make the equipment. So technically we could bypass SUP5 and buy a lot of these parts from the 
original equipment manufacturer. However, that means we could significantly reduce our spend with SUP5 
and if we're trying to maintain some level of power with an influence we want to then continue to have a 
reasonable spend with them. If we drop $8 or $10 million article spend with them every year because we buy 
spare parts from the manufacturer, BF is probably doing ourselves injustice in the longer term. Yet we know 
they can put the 200% markup on some parts. So, it is a challenging market I guess with interesting 
opportunities both for that and for us, because we could decide to change over that approach. But that also 
might have an impact on the warranties that we have on the certain pieces of equipment. So, provide parts 
who have other vendors in defeating it might affect the warranty issued something happens in equipment so 
it's quite a larger scale of decisions that need to happen. So, let's say they are the strongest trading partner 
in this relationship, so they have the most power.

There are some examples, when we work on new digital initiatives where we try and sort of reduce the 
maintenance on the rigs, we look into how we can use data sensors to take information, and that is then 
sensitive data because they could in turn use that data to improve the efficiency within to sell it to other 
customers. If we manage to gain something from it. So, it's about rights, who owns that data. If it's a joint 
collaboration, then technically you bought more than the data you might want it to be 50-50% or might be 
different percentages. But does that mean that either party has the rights, then to use that to then gain profit 
from an alternative company or competitor. So, they are quite open to collaborating and sharing in how we 
work together. So that also fosters more trust because it's a bit of a win-win because they're also trying to 
improve the efficiencies of what they do. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Okay. And as I understand, they are very open in information sharing and knowledge sharing. But you also 
mentioned earlier that when it comes to for example, an initiative that would help both companies grow or 
reduce their costs, they might still decline such an initiative because they can still get profit from other 
customers that they have. So then how would you describe this collaboration between BF and SUP5 over the 
years? Has it improved over the years, or is it worsening? And how would you then connect this to the 
openness and trust that has been developed over time? 
 
G. M.   
I don't think it has decreased the relationship or the quality of that. One example would be spare parts where 
we can purchase them directly from the equipment manufacturers. In some cases, have 200% of bpos price 
for that part that we do when we buy from SUP5 so that is a significant markup and when we approach SUP5 
about that, the parts price remains the same. They apply a fixed percentage discount to all the parts that BF 
buys from them. So, if it was a true collaboration and partnership, we would be able to source those parts 
from the original equipment manufacturers, without affecting our total spend with SUP5 or our larger 
relationship or without affecting the warranties. But that's not feasible, because they want as much money as 
possible from BF. We also need to maintain a level of prioritization and we want to be one of their preferred 
customers. So, we've got a way that spend in spare parts, each year, or when we have yard stays because 
we buy it from multiple sources. And that certainly affects relationships so they're using their power there to 
make sure that we do, stick with them, but they're also becoming more flexible. When we do a five-year SPS 
project, we don't know which parts we're going to need to use on SUP5 equipment, but we know we're going 
to overhaul let’s just say five pieces of equipment. So now the new approach that we've adopted recently 
has enabled us or SUP5 to supply us a whole kit for each of these five pieces of equipment. But then we can 
return whatever we don't use. That means when the job starts, we won't be short of any of the spare parts 
because we have everything potentially required for the job. And then whatever we don't use we can send 
back. That didn't use to be possible. So, they are opening up to more flexible ways of working. That then 
makes our lives easier, because we when we go to overhaul a part, we know we have all the spare parts 
there. And that won't be the cause of delaying the rig getting out to the yard that it's in which then can affect 
when we get back on and day-rate. So, the collaboration is certainly improving and being beneficial to both. 
 
ANNA-MARIA STAVRAKAKI  
Thank you. Now, moving one the next questions are about the overall supply chain collaboration and the 
supply chain performance. So, how frequently do you communicate with SUP5?  
 
Commented [AS127]: Trust on Power: 
 
The buyer’s trust on the supplier has not helped them 
reduce the prices for the equipment that they purchase 
from the supplier. 
 
The buyer tries to maintain a high amount of spend with 
this suppler to obtain favorable treatment and to limit 
the supplier’s use of power. 
 
Now the supplier is more open and flexible in new ways 
of working, because of the trust and the collaboration 
efforts of the buyer. For example when there is an SPS 
(special periodic survey), and the buyer is not 100% sure 
which parts will be needed, the supplier has offered the 
possibility to provide full kits in the beginning and then 
the buyer has the chance to return what was not used. 
This has been a huge help, because waiting for parts no 
longer delays the rig getting out of the yards to get 
back on a day-rate. 
 
Power on Trust: 
 
The supplier exerts power over the buyer regarding 
pricing and spend, making sure that the buyer “sticks 
with them”. The supplier is setting the boundaries in the 
collaboration, but those actions haven’t decreased trust 
or the quality of the relationship. 
 
 
Evolution of collaboration and trust in the relationship: 
 
It is not a true collaboration, because if it was, the buyer 
company would still be able to buy parts from the 
original equipment manufacturers without affecting the 
total spend with SUP5 or their larger relationship.  
But Collaboration is definitely improving and being 
beneficial for both.
